        <meta property="og:url" content="https://pubs.acs.org/doi/full/10.1021/acs.nanolett.0c02595"><meta property="og:title" content="Building Fast Diffusion Channel by Constructing Metal Sulfide/Metal Selenide Heterostructures for High-Performance Sodium Ion Batteries Anode"><meta property="og:image" content="https://pubs.acs.org/cms/10.1021/acs.nanolett.0c02595/asset/images/acs.nanolett.0c02595.social.jpeg_v03"><meta property="og:description" content="Heterostructure engineering is one of the most promising modification strategies toward improving sluggish kinetics for the anode of sodium ion batteries (SIBs). Herein, we report a systemic investigation on the different types of heterostructure interfaces’ effects of discharging products (Na2O, Na2S, Na2Se) on the rate performance. First-principle calculations reveal that the Na2S/Na2Se interface possesses the lowest diffusion energy barrier (0.39 eV) of Na among three kinds of interface structures (Na2O/Na2S, Na2O/Na2Se, and Na2S/Na2Se) due to its smallest recorded interface deformation, similar electronegativity, and lattice constant. The experimental evidence confirms that the metal sulfide/metal selenide (SnS/SnSe2) hierarchical anode exhibits outstanding rate performance, where the normalized capacity at 10 A g–1 compared to 0.1 A g–1 is 45.6%. The proposed design strategy in this work is helpful to design high rate performance anodes for advanced battery systems."><meta property="og:type" content="Article"><meta property="og:site_name" content="ACS Publications"><meta name="twitter:card" content="summary_large_image"><meta name="twitter:site" content="@ACSPublications"><meta name="twitter:image" content="https://pubs.acs.org/cms/10.1021/acs.nanolett.0c02595/asset/images/acs.nanolett.0c02595.social.jpeg_v03">

<!-- loading Javascript for using the persistence Service -->
    <script src="/wro/1122814~thiss-ds.js" async="" nonce="93187e55dd07075b-SJC"></script>

    <script type="text/javascript" nonce="93187e55dd07075b-SJC">

        var SeamlessAccessService = {
            spUrl: "/action/ssostart",
            returnURL: window.location.href,
            my_context: 'seamlessaccess.org',
            timeout: 5000,

            getWAYFLessUrl: function (entityId) {
                return this.spUrl + "?idp=" + encodeURIComponent(entityId)
                    + "&redirectUri=" + encodeURIComponent(this.returnURL);
            },
            retrieveEntity: function (ms) {
                var self = this;
                var persistService = new thiss.PersistenceService('https://service.seamlessaccess.org/ps/');

                if (ms == undefined || ms === '') {
                    ms = this.timeout;
                }

                return new Promise(function (resolve, reject) {
                    persistService.entities(self.my_context)
                        .then(function (res) {
                            return resolve(res.data);
                        }, function (err) {
                            // failed
                            reject('failed to retrieve entity from local storage: ' + err);
                        });
                    // Set up the timeout
                    setTimeout(function () {
                        reject('Promise timed out ' + ms + ' ms');
                    }, ms);
                });
            },
            updateEntity: function (entitydata) {
                var self = this;
                var persistService = new thiss.PersistenceService('https://service.seamlessaccess.org/ps/');

                var jsonObj = {};
                try {
                    //try to parse it, if it is a json string
                    jsonObj = JSON.parse(entitydata.val());
                } catch (e) {
                    jsonObj = entitydata;
                }
                return new Promise(function (resolve, reject) {
                    persistService.update(self.my_context, jsonObj)
                        .then(function (res) {
                            // Process the results
                            var ssoInstitutions = res.data;
                            return resolve(ssoInstitutions);
                        }, function (err) {
                            // failed
                            console.log('failed to update the local storage due to: ' + err);
                            reject(err);
                        });
                })
            },
            removeEntity: function (entityId) {
                var self = this;
                var persistService = new thiss.PersistenceService('https://service.seamlessaccess.org/ps/');

                return new Promise(function (resolve) {
                    persistService.remove(self.my_context, entityId)
                        .then(function (res) {
                            return resolve(res);
                        }, function (err) {
                            // failed
                            console.log('failed to remove from local storage duo to: ' + err);
                        });
                })
            },
            getAccessibleEntityStatus: function (doi, entityId, entityTitle) {
                return new Promise(function (resolve, reject) {
                    $.ajax({
                        type: 'POST',
                        url: '/action/seamlessAccess',
                        accept: {
                            text: "application/json"
                        },
                        data: {
                            doi: doi,
                            entityid: entityId,
                            entityTitle: entityTitle,
                            eventGroupKey: '65686003-7120-437d-be91-fd0019fd98fb'
                        }

                    }).done(function (status) {
                        return resolve(status);
                    }).fail(function (status) {
                        return reject(status);
                    });
                });
            },
            getEntitiesWithAllInfo: function (doi) {
                let self = this,
                    arrayOfPromises = [];
                return new Promise(function (resolve, reject) {
                    self.retrieveEntity().then(function (res) {
                        let allSsoInstitutions = res;
                        for (i = 0; i < allSsoInstitutions.length; i++) {
                            let ssoInstitution = allSsoInstitutions[i];
                            arrayOfPromises.push(new Promise(function (resolve) {
                                self.getAccessibleEntityStatus(doi, ssoInstitution.entity.entityID, ssoInstitution.entity.title)
                                    .then(function (entityStatus) {
                                        resolve({
                                            entityID: ssoInstitution.entity.entityID,
                                            entityTitle: ssoInstitution.entity.title,
                                            entityStatusObj: entityStatus,
                                            entitySsoUrl: self.getWAYFLessUrl(ssoInstitution.entity.entityID)
                                        });
                                    }, function (err) {
                                        console.log('failed to get the entity status: ' + ssoInstitution.entity.entityID,err);
                                    });
                            }));
                        }
                        if(allSsoInstitutions.length === 0 && true){
                            self.getAccessibleEntityStatus(doi, null, null)
                                .then(function () {
                                    console.log('No Entity found');
                                }, function (err) {
                                    console.log('failed to get the none entity status' ,err);
                                });
                        }
                        Promise.all(arrayOfPromises)
                            .then(function (finalResponse) {
                                resolve(finalResponse);
                            }).catch(function (err) {
                                reject(err);
                            });

                    }).catch(function (err) {
                        reject(err);
                    })
                });
            }
        };

        window.addEventListener('load', function () {
            document.querySelectorAll(".sso-institution").forEach(function (institution) {
                var entityId = institution.getAttribute('data-entityid');
                var entityName = institution.getAttribute('data-name');
                var entitydata = {
                    entityID: entityId,
                    title: entityName
                };
                institution.addEventListener("click", function (e) {
                    e.preventDefault();

                    if ($('.institution-preference-userconsent-checkbox').length > 0) {
                        let institutionPreferenceUserConsentCheckbox = $('.institution-preference-userconsent-checkbox').get(0);
                        if (!$(institutionPreferenceUserConsentCheckbox).is(":checked")) {
                            return;
                        }
                    }

                    var self = this;
                    SeamlessAccessService.updateEntity(entitydata).then(function (res) {
                        console.log(res);
                        window.location.href = self.href;
                    }).catch(function (err) {
                        window.location.href = self.href;
                    });
                });
            });
        });

    </script>

type="text/javascript" src="/specs/products/achs/releasedAssets/public/mathjax-0a9852ad89643b94fe5099b753f6a294/MathJax.js?config=TeX-AMS-MML_HTMLorMML"></script><script type="text/x-mathjax-config;executed=true">MathJax.Hub.Config({
            menuSettings: {
                zoom: "Click",
                zscale: "150%"
            },
            SVG: {
                fonts: ["STIX-Web"],
                linebreaks: { automatic: true },
            },
            "HTML-CSS": {
                fonts: ["STIX"],
                linebreaks: { automatic: true },
                scale: 90,
            },
        });

        MathJax.Hub.Register.StartupHook("mml Jax Ready", function () {
            var MML = MathJax.ElementJax.mml;
            MML.mi.prototype.setTeXclass = function (prev) {
                this.getPrevClass(prev);
                return this;
            };
            MML.mfrac.Augment({
                setTeXclass: function (prev) {
                    this.getPrevClass(prev);
                    for (var i = 0, m = this.data.length; i < m; i++) {
                        if (this.data[i]) this.data[i].setTeXclass();
                    }
                    return this;
                }
            });
        });

        MathJax.Hub.Register.StartupHook("SVG Jax Ready", function () {
            var FONT = MathJax.OutputJax.SVG.FONTDATA.VARIANT["-STIX-Web-variant"] || MathJax.OutputJax.SVG.FONTDATA.VARIANT["-STIX-variant"];
            delete MathJax.ElementJax.mml.mo.prototype.remapChars['°'];
            if (FONT) {
                FONT.remap[0xB0]= [0x25E6, "normal"];
                FONT.remap[0x2A]= [0x2217, "normal"];
            }

        });

        MathJax.Hub.Register.StartupHook("MathML Jax Ready", function () {
            var PARSE = MathJax.InputJax.MathML.Parse;
            var ADD = PARSE.prototype.AddChildren;
            PARSE.Augment({
                AddChildren: function (mml, node) {
                    ADD.call(this, mml, node);
                    if (mml.type === 'mrow') {
                        var first = mml.data[0], last = mml.data[mml.data.length-1];
                        if (mml.open && !mml.data[0].Get('stretchy')) delete mml.open;
                        if (mml.close && !mml.data[mml.data.length - 1].Get('stretchy')) delete mml.close;
                    }
                }
            });
        });

        MathJax.Hub.Register.StartupHook("HTML-CSS Jax Ready", function () {
            var HTMLCSS = MathJax.OutputJax['HTML-CSS'];
            var DELIMITERS = HTMLCSS.FONTDATA.DELIMITERS;
            var FONT = HTMLCSS.FONTDATA.VARIANT["-STIX-variant"] || HTMLCSS.FONTDATA.VARIANT["-STIX-Web-variant"]

            var MML = MathJax.ElementJax.mml;

            MML.mrow.Augment({
              _HTMLlineBreaks: MML.mrow.prototype.HTMLlineBreaks,
              HTMLlineBreaks: function (span) {

                if (this.parent.linebreakContainer && this.Get("display") === "inline") return false;
                return this._HTMLlineBreaks(span);
              }
            });


            DELIMITERS[0x0305] = {dir: "H", alias: 0x00AF};
            DELIMITERS[0x033F] = {dir: "H", alias: 0x003D};
            DELIMITERS[0x20E1] = {dir: "H", alias: 0x2194};

            if (FONT) {
                FONT.remap[0xB0]= [0x25E6, "normal"];
                FONT.remap[0x2A]= [0x2217, "normal"];
            }

            delete MathJax.ElementJax.mml.mo.prototype.remapChars['°'];
        });

        MathJax.Hub.Register.StartupHook("HTML-CSS multiline Ready", function () {
            var HTMLCSS = MathJax.OutputJax["HTML-CSS"];
            var MML = MathJax.ElementJax.mml;
            var PENALTY = MML.mbase.prototype.HTMLlinebreakPenalty;

            MML.msqrt.Augment({
                linebreakContainer: false,
                HTMLbetterBreak: function () {return false}
            });
            MML.mroot.Augment({
                linebreakContainer: false,
                HTMLbetterBreak: function () {return false}
            });
            MML.mfrac.Augment({
                linebreakContainer: false,
                HTMLbetterBreak: function () {return false}
            });
            MML.mspace.Augment({
                defaultDef: {
                    indentalign: MML.INDENTALIGN.AUTO,
                    indentshift: "0",
                    indenttarget: "",
                    indentalignfirst: MML.INDENTALIGN.INDENTALIGN,
                    indentshiftfirst: MML.INDENTSHIFT.INDENTSHIFT,
                    indentalignlast: MML.INDENTALIGN.INDENTALIGN,
                    indentshiftlast: MML.INDENTSHIFT.INDENTSHIFT
                },
                HTMLbetterBreak: function (info,state) {
                    if (info.values && info.values.id === this.spanID) {return false}
                    var values = this.getValues(
                        "linebreak",
                        "indentalign","indentshift",
                        "indentalignfirst","indentshiftfirst",
                        "indentalignlast","indentshiftlast"
                    );
                    var linebreakValue = values.linebreak;
                    if (!linebreakValue || this.hasDimAttr()) {
                        linebreakValue = MML.LINEBREAK.AUTO;
                    }
                    var W = info.scanW, span = this.HTMLspanElement(), w = span.bbox.w;
                    if (span.style.paddingLeft) {w += HTMLCSS.unEm(span.style.paddingLeft)}
                    if (W - info.shift === 0) {return false} // don't break at zero width
                    var offset = HTMLCSS.linebreakWidth - W;

                    if (state.n === 0 && (values.indentshiftfirst !== state.VALUES.indentshiftfirst ||
                        values.indentalignfirst !== state.VALUES.indentalignfirst)) {
                        var align = this.HTMLgetAlign(state,values),
                            shift = this.HTMLgetShift(state,values,align);
                        offset += (info.shift - shift);
                    }

                    var penalty = Math.floor(offset / HTMLCSS.linebreakWidth * 1000);
                    if (penalty < 0) {penalty = PENALTY.toobig - 3*penalty}
                    penalty += info.nest * PENALTY.nestfactor;

                    var linebreak = PENALTY[linebreakValue]||0;
                    if (linebreakValue === MML.LINEBREAK.AUTO && w >= PENALTY.spacelimit &&
                        !this.mathbackground && !this.background)
                    {linebreak = [(w+PENALTY.spaceoffset)*PENALTY.spacefactor]}
                    if (!MathJax.Object.isArray(linebreak)) {
                        if (linebreak || offset >= 0) {penalty = linebreak * info.nest}
                    } else {penalty = Math.max(1,penalty + linebreak[0] * info.nest)}

                    if (penalty >= info.penalty) {return false}
                    info.penalty = penalty; info.values = values; info.W = W; info.w = w;
                    values.lineleading = state.VALUES.lineleading;
                    values.linebreakstyle = "before"; values.id = this.spanID;
                    return true;
                }
            });


            
            PENALTY.goodbreak = [-400];

        });</script><style type="text/css">.MathJax_Hover_Frame {border-radius: .25em; -webkit-border-radius: .25em; -moz-border-radius: .25em; -khtml-border-radius: .25em; box-shadow: 0px 0px 15px #83A; -webkit-box-shadow: 0px 0px 15px #83A; -moz-box-shadow: 0px 0px 15px #83A; -khtml-box-shadow: 0px 0px 15px #83A; border: 1px solid #A6D ! important; display: inline-block; position: absolute}

<script type="text/javascript" nonce="93187e55dd07075b-SJC">
var dfpReady = false;
$(document).ready(function() {
    // var journalTitle = 'nano_letters';
    var taxonomyKeywords = $('.article_header-taxonomy');
    var articleKeyword = $('meta[name="keywords"]');
    var articleTitle = $('meta[name="dc.Title"]');
    var articleDescription = $('meta[name="dc.Description"]');
    var abstractKeyword = $('p.articleBody_abstractText:contains("Keywords: ")');
    var searchTerms = [];

    if (taxonomyKeywords.length > 0) {
        $(taxonomyKeywords).children(".rlist--inline").children("li").each(function() {
            searchTerms.push($(this).text());
        });
    }
    else if (articleKeyword.length > 0) { 
        searchTerms = searchTerms.concat(articleKeyword.prop('content').split(', '));
    }
    else if (abstractKeyword.length > 0) {
        searchTerms = searchTerms.concat(abstractKeyword.text().split("Keywords: ")[1].split('; '));
    }
    else if (articleDescription.length > 0) {
        $(articleDescription).each(function() {
            articleDescriptionKeywords = $(this).prop('content');
            articleDescriptionKeywords = articleDescriptionKeywords.replace(/.,/g,"");
            articleDescriptionKeywords = articleDescriptionKeywords.replace(/\s{2,}/g," ");
            searchTerms = searchTerms.concat(articleDescriptionKeywords.split(' '));
        });
    }

    if (articleTitle.length > 0) {
        searchTerms = searchTerms.concat(articleTitle.prop('content').split(' '));
    }

    googletag.cmd.push(function() {
        var mappingLeader = googletag.sizeMapping().
        addSize([750, 200], [728, 90]). 
        addSize([0, 0], [320, 50]).
        build();
        //leaderboard
        googletag.defineSlot('/8868/journal/nano_letters/article', [728, 90], 'dfp-journal-article-leaderboard').defineSizeMapping(mappingLeader).setTargeting('location', 'leaderboard1').setTargeting('Keyword', searchTerms).addService(googletag.pubads());
        //recommend
        googletag.defineSlot("/8868/journal/nano_letters/article", [1, 1], "dfp-journal-article-recommend").setTargeting("ntvPlacement", ["1100167"]).setTargeting("location", "recommend").setTargeting('Keyword', searchTerms).addService(googletag.pubads());
        //googletag.pubads().enableSingleRequest();
        googletag.pubads().collapseEmptyDivs();
        googletag.pubads().setTargeting('inst',dartInstitutionType).setTargeting('memb',dartMember);
        googletag.pubads().addEventListener("slotRenderEnded", function(event) {
            console.log(event.slot.getSlotElementId() + " isEmpty: " + event.isEmpty + " | creative id: " + event.creativeId + " " + event.size[0] + "x" + event.size[1]);
            if((event.isEmpty === false) && (event.slot.getSlotElementId() != "dfp-journal-article-recommend")) {
                $("#" + event.slot.getSlotElementId()).parents(".advertisement").slideDown();
                console.log("ad display: " + event.creativeId + " " + event.size[0] + "x" + event.size[1]);
            }
            // else {
            //     console.log("no ad found");
            // }
        });
        googletag.enableServices();
        dfpReady = true;
    });
});
</script>

class="icon-check-circle"></i><span><strong>Cite This:</strong></span></a><span class="article_header-cite-this figure-viewer-cite-this"><span class="cit-fg-title"><i>Nano Lett.</i></span><span <script nonce="93187e55dd07075b-SJC">
$(function() {
    var serialCode = "nalefd";
    var books = ["reagents", "symposium", "advances", "medichemreviews", "styleguide"]
    if(books.includes(serialCode) === false) {
        // default click action
        $("[data-id]").on("mousedown", function(e) {
            var currentClickID = $(this).attr("data-id");

            if(currentClickID.includes("_hover") === false) {
                if(currentClickID.match("^article_") && !currentClickID.match("^article_abstract_CC")) {   
                    gtag('event', "Journal Article", { "Article_Title": "Building Fast Diffusion Channel by Constructing Metal Sulfide/Metal Selenide Heterostructures for High-Performance Sodium Ion Batteries Anode", "Article_DOI": "10.1021/acs.nanolett.0c02595", "Article_Element": $(this).attr("data-id") });
                }
            }
        });
        
        // Access Management icons hover
        var articleAccessManagementIconHover = false;
        var articleAccessManagementIconHoverEvent = false;
        $("[data-id='article_access_icon']").on("mouseenter", function(e) {
            if(articleAccessManagementIconHoverEvent === false) {
                articleAccessManagementIconHover = true;
                var articleAccessManagementHover =  $(this).attr("data-id") + "_hover";
                
                setTimeout(function() { 
                    if(articleAccessManagementIconHover === true) {
                        gtag("event", "Journal Article", { "Article_Title": "Building Fast Diffusion Channel by Constructing Metal Sulfide/Metal Selenide Heterostructures for High-Performance Sodium Ion Batteries Anode", "Article_DOI": "10.1021/acs.nanolett.0c02595", "Article_Element": articleAccessManagementHover });
    
                        articleAccessManagementIconHoverEvent = true;
                    }
                }, 1000);
            }
        });
        $("[data-id='article_access_icon']").on("mouseleave", function(e) {
            articleAccessManagementIconHover = false;
        });
    
        // articleRightsPermissionsLink hover
        var articleRightsPermissionsLinkHover = false;
        var articleRightsPermissionsLinkHoverEvent = false;
        $("[data-id='article_rights_permissions']").on("mouseenter", function(e) {
            if(articleRightsPermissionsLinkHoverEvent === false) {
                articleRightsPermissionsLinkHover = true;
                var articleRightsHover =  $(this).attr("data-id") + "_hover";
                
                setTimeout(function() { 
                    if(articleRightsPermissionsLinkHover === true) {
                        gtag("event", "Journal Article", { "Article_Title": "Building Fast Diffusion Channel by Constructing Metal Sulfide/Metal Selenide Heterostructures for High-Performance Sodium Ion Batteries Anode", "Article_DOI": "10.1021/acs.nanolett.0c02595", "Article_Element": articleRightsHover });
    
                        articleRightsPermissionsLinkHoverEvent = true;
                    }
                }, 1000);
            }
        });
        $("[data-id='article_rights_permissions']").on("mouseleave", function(e) {
            articleRightsPermissionsLinkHover = false;
        });
    
        // Author hover
        var articleAuthorHover = false
        var articleAuthorHoverEvent = false
        $("[data-id='article_author_info']").on("mouseenter", function(e) {
            if((articleAuthorHoverEvent === false) && ($(this).parent().hasClass("view-affs") === false)) {
                articleAuthorHover = true;
                var authorInfoHover =  $(this).attr("data-id") + "_hover";
                
                setTimeout(function() { 
                    if((articleAuthorHover === true) && (articleAuthorHoverEvent === false)) {
                        gtag("event", "Journal Article", { "Article_Title": "Building Fast Diffusion Channel by Constructing Metal Sulfide/Metal Selenide Heterostructures for High-Performance Sodium Ion Batteries Anode", "Article_DOI": "10.1021/acs.nanolett.0c02595", "Article_Element": authorInfoHover });
    
                        articleAuthorHoverEvent = true;
                    }
                }, 1000);
            }
        });
        $("[data-id='article_author_info']").on("mouseleave", function(e) {
            articleAuthorHover = false;
        });
    
        // CC License hover
        var articleAbstractCCHover = false
        var articleAbstractCCHoverEvent = false
        var articleDrawerCCHover = false
        var articleDrawerCCHoverEvent = false
        $(".article__cc-license__inter a").on("mouseenter", function(e) {
            if($(this).attr("data-id")) {
                if(($(this).attr("data-id") === "article_drawer_CC-License") && (articleDrawerCCHoverEvent === false)) {
                    articleDrawerCCHover = true;
                    
                    setTimeout(function() { 
                        if((articleDrawerCCHover === true) && (articleDrawerCCHoverEvent === false)) {
                            gtag("event", "Journal Article", { "Article_Title": "Building Fast Diffusion Channel by Constructing Metal Sulfide/Metal Selenide Heterostructures for High-Performance Sodium Ion Batteries Anode", "Article_DOI": "10.1021/acs.nanolett.0c02595", "Article_Element": "article_drawer_CC-License_hover" });
    
                            articleDrawerCCHoverEvent = true;
                        }
                    }, 1000);
                }
                else if(($(this).attr("data-id").match("^article_abstract_CC")) && (articleAbstractCCHoverEvent === false)) {
                    articleAbstractCCHover = true;
    
                    setTimeout(function() { 
                        if((articleAbstractCCHover === true) && (articleAbstractCCHoverEvent === false)) {
                            gtag("event", "Journal Article", { "Article_Title": "Building Fast Diffusion Channel by Constructing Metal Sulfide/Metal Selenide Heterostructures for High-Performance Sodium Ion Batteries Anode", "Article_DOI": "10.1021/acs.nanolett.0c02595", "Article_Element": "article_abstract_CC_hover" });
    
                            articleAbstractCCHoverEvent = true;
                        }
                    }, 1000);
                }
            }
            else if(($(this).hasClass("article__cc-license__link")) && (articleAbstractCCHoverEvent === false)) {
                articleAbstractCCHover = true;
                
                setTimeout(function() { 
                    if((articleAbstractCCHover === true) && (articleAbstractCCHoverEvent === false)) {
                        gtag("event", "Journal Article", { "Article_Title": "Building Fast Diffusion Channel by Constructing Metal Sulfide/Metal Selenide Heterostructures for High-Performance Sodium Ion Batteries Anode", "Article_DOI": "10.1021/acs.nanolett.0c02595", "Article_Element": "article_abstract_CC_hover" });
    
                        articleAbstractCCHoverEvent = true;
                    }
                }, 1000);
            }
        });
        $(".article__cc-license__inter a").on("mouseleave", function(e) {
            if(articleAbstractCCHover === true) {
                articleAbstractCCHover = false;
            }
            if(articleDrawerCCHover = true) {
                articleDrawerCCHover = false;
            }
        });
        
        // show recommeneded click delay
        var intervalCount = 0;
        var showRecommendedInterval = setInterval(function() {
            intervalCount += 100;
            if($(".show-recommended__wrapper .show-recommended").length > 0) {
                clearInterval(showRecommendedInterval);
                $("[data-id='article_drawer_RecommendedShowMore']").on("mousedown", function(e) {
                    gtag('event', "Journal Article", { "Article_Title": "Building Fast Diffusion Channel by Constructing Metal Sulfide/Metal Selenide Heterostructures for High-Performance Sodium Ion Batteries Anode", "Article_DOI": "10.1021/acs.nanolett.0c02595", "Article_Element": $(this).attr("data-id") });
                });
                $("[data-id='article_drawer_RecommendedShowLess']").on("mousedown", function(e) {
                    gtag('event', "Journal Article", { "Article_Title": "Building Fast Diffusion Channel by Constructing Metal Sulfide/Metal Selenide Heterostructures for High-Performance Sodium Ion Batteries Anode", "Article_DOI": "10.1021/acs.nanolett.0c02595", "Article_Element": $(this).attr("data-id") });
                });
            }
        }, 100);
    }
});
</script>

Find more information on<a href="https://help.altmetric.com/support/solutions/articles/6000233311-how-is-the-altmetric-attention-score-calculated"> the Altmetric Attention Score and how the score is calculated.</a></p></div></div></div></div></div></div></div></div><div class="article_abstract"><div class="article_abstract-title" id="Abstract" tabindex="0"><h2>Abstract</h2><div class="article__copy"><button class="article__copy__btn"><i class="article__copy__icon icon-copy-solid" aria-hidden="true"></i></button><span class="article__copy__text">Click to copy section link</span><span class="article__copy__success">Section link copied!</span></div></div><div class="article_abstract-content hlFld-Abstract" id="abstractBox" property="description"><figure id="_i1" data-index="0" class="article__inlineFigure article_abstract-img"><button class="figure-viewer__trigger" aria-label="open figure viewer"><img class="inline-fig internalNav" src="/cms/10.1021/acs.nanolett.0c02595/asset/images/medium/nl0c02595_0005.gif" alt="" id="tgr1" property="image" style="width:405px; height:275px"></button><div class="figure-bottom-links"><div class="download-hi-res-img"><a title="High Resolution Image" href="/cms/10.1021/acs.nanolett.0c02595/asset/images/large/nl0c02595_0005.jpeg"><span>High Resolution Image</span></a></div><div class="download-ppt"><a title="Download MS PowerPoint Slide" href="/action/downloadFigures?doi=10.1021/acs.nanolett.0c02595&amp;id=_i1"><span>Download MS PowerPoint Slide</span></a></div></div></figure><p class="articleBody_abstractText">Heterostructure engineering is one of the most promising modification strategies toward improving sluggish kinetics for the anode of sodium ion batteries (SIBs). Herein, we report a systemic investigation on the different types of heterostructure interfaces’ effects of discharging products (Na<sub>2</sub>O, Na<sub>2</sub>S, Na<sub>2</sub>Se) on the rate performance. First-principle calculations reveal that the Na<sub>2</sub>S/Na<sub>2</sub>Se interface possesses the lowest diffusion energy barrier (0.39 eV) of Na among three kinds of interface structures (Na<sub>2</sub>O/Na<sub>2</sub>S, Na<sub>2</sub>O/Na<sub>2</sub>Se, and Na<sub>2</sub>S/Na<sub>2</sub>Se) due to its smallest recorded interface deformation, similar electronegativity, and lattice constant. The experimental evidence confirms that the metal sulfide/metal selenide (SnS/SnSe<sub>2</sub>) hierarchical anode exhibits outstanding rate performance, where the normalized capacity at 10 A g<sup>–1</sup> compared to 0.1 A g<sup>–1</sup> is 45.6%. The proposed design strategy in this work is helpful to design high rate performance anodes for advanced battery systems.</p></div><div class="article__cc-license"><div class="article__cc-license__body"><p class="article__cc-license__para">This publication is licensed under the terms of your
                            institutional subscription.
                            <a href="/servlet/linkout?type=rightslink&amp;url=startPage%3D6199%26pageCount%3D7%26copyright%3DAmerican%2BChemical%2BSociety%26author%3DTianshuai%2BWang%252C%2BDominik%2BLegut%252C%2BYanchen%2BFan%252C%2Bet%2Bal%26orderBeanReset%3Dtrue%26imprint%3DAmerican%2BChemical%2BSociety%26volumeNum%3D20%26issueNum%3D8%26contentID%3Dacs.nanolett.0c02595%26title%3DBuilding%2BFast%2BDiffusion%2BChannel%2Bby%2BConstructing%2BMetal%2BSulfide%252FMetal%2BSelenide%2BHeterostructures%2Bfor%2BHigh-Performance%2BSodium%2BIon%2BBatteries%2BAnode%26numPages%3D7%26pa%3D%26issn%3D1530-6984%26publisherName%3Dacs%26publication%3Dnalefd%26rpt%3Dn%26endPage%3D6205%26publicationDate%3DAugust%2B2020" class="link--bold">Request reuse permissions.</a></p></div></div><span class="hidden" property="publisher">ACS Publications</span><div class="article_header-article-copyright">Copyright © 2020 American Chemical Society</div><div class="article_header-footer-right"><div class="articleHeaderDropzone7" data-pb-dropzone="articleHeaderDropzone7"><!-- Empty dropzone --></div><div class="article__tags article__tags--subjects"><div class="article__tags__container"><header class="article__tags__header"><h3 class="article__tags__heading">Subjects</h3><div tabindex="0" class="article__tags__tooltip"><div aria-describedby="tooltip-subjects" class="article__tags__icon"><i class="icon-info-circle-outline"></i><span class="sr-only">what are subjects</span></div><p id="tooltip-subjects" role="tooltip" class="article__tags__diff">
                                        Article subjects are automatically applied from the ACS Subject Taxonomy and describe the scientific concepts and themes of the article.</p></div></header><ul class="article__tags__list"><li class="article__tags__item"><a class="article__tags__link" href="/action/doSearch?ConceptID=291517&amp;ref=fulltext" title="Batteries"> Batteries </a></li><li class="article__tags__item"><a class="article__tags__link" href="/action/doSearch?ConceptID=291331&amp;ref=fulltext" title="Composites"> Composites </a></li><li class="article__tags__item"><a class="article__tags__link" href="/action/doSearch?ConceptID=290830&amp;ref=fulltext" title="Diffusion"> Diffusion </a></li><li class="article__tags__item"><a class="article__tags__link" href="/action/doSearch?ConceptID=291371&amp;ref=fulltext" title="Electrodes"> Electrodes </a></li><li class="article__tags__item"><a class="article__tags__link" href="/action/doSearch?ConceptID=290817&amp;ref=fulltext" title="Interfaces"> Interfaces </a></li></ul></div></div></div><div class="article__tags article__tags--keywords hlFld-Abstract"><div class="article__tags__container"><header class="article__tags__header"><h3 class="article__tags__heading">Keywords</h3><div class="article__tags__tooltip" tabindex="0"><div class="article__tags__icon" aria-describedby="tooltip-keywords"><i class="icon-info-circle-outline"></i><span class="sr-only">what are keywords</span></div><p class="article__tags__diff" id="tooltip-keywords" role="tooltip">
                        Article keywords are supplied by the authors and highlight key terms and topics
                        of the paper.
                    </p></div></header><ul class="article__tags__list"><li class="article__tags__item"><a class="article__tags__link" href="https://pubs.acs.org/action/doSearch?action=search&amp;AllField=Fast+Diffusion+Channel&amp;qsSearchArea=AllField&amp;ref=fulltext" data-id="article_abstract_Keyword_Fast%20diffusion%20channel">Fast diffusion channel</a></li><li class="article__tags__item"> <a class="article__tags__link" href="https://pubs.acs.org/action/doSearch?action=search&amp;AllField=Heterostructures+Anode+Materials&amp;qsSearchArea=AllField&amp;ref=fulltext" data-id="article_abstract_Keyword_heterostructures%20anode%20materials">heterostructures anode materials</a></li><li class="article__tags__item"> <a class="article__tags__link" href="https://pubs.acs.org/action/doSearch?action=search&amp;AllField=Metal+Sulfide%2Fmetal+Selenide&amp;qsSearchArea=AllField&amp;ref=fulltext" data-id="article_abstract_Keyword_metal%20sulfide%2Fmetal%20selenide">metal sulfide/metal selenide</a></li><li class="article__tags__item"> <a class="article__tags__link" href="https://pubs.acs.org/action/doSearch?action=search&amp;AllField=Sodium+Ion+Batteries&amp;qsSearchArea=AllField&amp;ref=fulltext" data-id="article_abstract_Keyword_sodium%20ion%20batteries">sodium ion batteries</a></li></ul></div></div></div><div class="article_content"><div class="article_content-table hlFld-FullText"><div class="article_content-row"><div class="article_content-left" property="articleBody" style="width: 384px;"><div class="extra-info-sec articleNote"><h4><i class="icon-note-flag" aria-hidden="true"></i>Note</h4><p class="last">Due to a production error, this paper was published ASAP on July 30, 2020, with the incorrect affiliation for corresponding author Jian Qin. The corrected version was reposted on July 31, 2020.</p></div><div id="sec1" class="NLM_sec NLM_sec_level_1"><div class="NLM_p first">Sustainable and clean energy is in great demand due to the need of continuous economic development. Of diverse renewable energy technologies, energy storage via battery technologies has shown great convenience in today’s society. <a href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref1 ref2 ref3 ref4" id="referenceLink" data-rid="ref1 ref2 ref3 ref4" aria-label="reference 1 to 4">(1−4)</a> Recently, sodium ion batteries (SIBs) have attracted increasing attention as a potential alternative electric energy storage technology because of the abundant reserves of the raw materials, <a href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref5 ref6 ref7" id="referenceLink" data-rid="ref5 ref6 ref7" aria-label="reference 5 to 7">(5−7)</a> low fabrication cost, <a href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref8 ref9 ref10" id="referenceLink" data-rid="ref8 ref9 ref10" aria-label="reference 8 to 10">(8−10)</a> and similar electrochemical properties compared with the lithium ion battery. <a href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref11 ref12 ref13 ref14" id="referenceLink" data-rid="ref11 ref12 ref13 ref14" aria-label="reference 11 to 14">(11−14)</a></div><div class="NLM_p">Nevertheless, there are still many challenges to realize the commercial application of SIBs. <a href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref15 ref16" id="referenceLink" data-rid="ref15 ref16" aria-label="reference 15 and 16">(15,16)</a> One of the key points is the lack of suitable anode materials. <a href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref17 ref18 ref19" id="referenceLink" data-rid="ref17 ref18 ref19" aria-label="reference 17 to 19">(17−19)</a> Conversion-type negative materials, which are mainly made up of transition-metal oxide/sulfide/selenide, show tremendous potential in SIBs due to their high theoretical specific capacity. The overall conversion reactions during the sodiation mechanism can be described as M<sub><i>a</i></sub>X<sub><i>b</i></sub> + (<i>bn</i>)Na<sup>+</sup> + (<i>bn</i>)e<sup>–</sup> ↔ <i>a</i>M + <i>b</i>Na<sub><i>n</i></sub>X) [<i>a</i> = transition metal, X = O, S, Se]. <a href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref20 ref21 ref22" id="referenceLink" data-rid="ref20 ref21 ref22" aria-label="reference 20 to 22">(20−22)</a> Benefiting from the development of composite technology, <a href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref23 ref24 ref25" id="referenceLink" data-rid="ref23 ref24 ref25" aria-label="reference 23 to 25">(23−25)</a> the problem of low electronic conductivity and huge expansion in intrinsic transition oxide/sulfide/selenide has been greatly improved by constructing heterostructures with carbon materials. <a href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref26 ref27" id="referenceLink" data-rid="ref26 ref27" aria-label="reference 26 and 27">(26,27)</a> However, the inherent large sodium ion radius still poses a major challenge in the design of high rate performance batteries. <a href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref28" id="referenceLink" data-rid="ref28" aria-label="reference 28">(28)</a> Previous work has demonstrated that creating heterostructures by coupling nanocrystals with different band gaps will enhance the surface reactions kinetics and facilitate charge transport due to the internal electric field of the heterointerfaces. <a href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref29 ref30 ref31 ref32" id="referenceLink" data-rid="ref29 ref30 ref31 ref32" aria-label="reference 29 to 32">(29−32)</a> However, these works primarily concentrate on building a high diffusion channel of the intrinsic electrode in the initial sodiation stage. It is known that the metal oxide/sulfide/selenide will convert to Na<sub>2</sub>O/Na<sub>2</sub>S/Na<sub>2</sub>Se in the cycling process. The investigation of the influence on the rate performance in the later sodiation process does not receive much attention.</div><div class="NLM_p last">Herein, we report a systematic investigation on the diffusion behavior of Na ions in the Na<sub>2</sub>O/Na<sub>2</sub>S, Na<sub>2</sub>O/Na<sub>2</sub>Se, and Na<sub>2</sub>S/Na<sub>2</sub>Se heterostructures. Based on the theoretical modeling, we find that the Na<sub>2</sub>S/Na<sub>2</sub>Se heterostructures show the best diffusion performance (∼0.39 eV) among the three interfacial structures. Such characteristics on high rate performance give foresight for the advantage of designing a good power density anode of SIBs by constructing metal sulfide/metal selenide composite materials. On the basis of theoretical design, we further synthesize the SnS/SnSe<sub>2</sub> heterostructures as the sodium anode. Experimental results demonstrated that this structure exhibits excellent rate performance and good reversible behavior.</div></div><div id="sec2" class="NLM_sec NLM_sec_level_1"><div class="article_content-header"><div class="article_content-header-row"><div class="article_content-title" tabindex="0"><h2 id="_i3">Results and Discussion</h2><div class="article__copy"><button class="article__copy__btn"><i class="article__copy__icon icon-copy-solid" aria-hidden="true"></i></button><span class="article__copy__text">Click to copy section link</span><span class="article__copy__success">Section link copied!</span></div></div></div></div><hr><div id="sec2_1" class="NLM_sec NLM_sec_level_2"><div id="ac_i4" class="anchor-spacer"></div><h3 class="article-section__title" id="_i4"> Theoretical Simulation</h3><div class="NLM_p">As illustrated in <a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig1" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure </a><a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig1" id="" class=" internalNav">1</a>a, the diffusion behavior of Na ion in the composite materials (graphene/metal oxide, sulfide, selenide) requires penetrating through the discharging product phases (Na<sub>2</sub>O, Na<sub>2</sub>S, and Na<sub>2</sub>Se). Thus, we first simulate the diffusivity of Na ion in these bulk phases. The atomic configurations of the primitive cell are shown in <a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig1" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure </a><a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig1" id="" class=" internalNav">1</a>b. The interstitial Na atom prefers to locate at the center of the octahedron zone composed of six nearby O/S/Se atoms due to the high symmetry of the Na<sub>2</sub>O/Na<sub>2</sub>S/Na<sub>2</sub>Se phase, which can endure the big radius of the Na atom. According to the calculation results, the diffusion energy barrier of the Na atom in the Na<sub>2</sub>O, Na<sub>2</sub>S, and Na<sub>2</sub>Se bulk phases is 0.45, 0.46, and 0.43 eV (<a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig1" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure </a><a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig1" id="" class=" internalNav">1</a>c), respectively.</div><figure id="fig1" data-index="1" class="article__inlineFigure"><h2 class="fig-label">Figure 1</h2><button class="figure-viewer__trigger" aria-label="open figure viewer"><img src="/cms/10.1021/acs.nanolett.0c02595/asset/images/medium/nl0c02595_0001.gif" alt="" data-lg-src="/cms/10.1021/acs.nanolett.0c02595/asset/images/large/nl0c02595_0001.jpeg" id="gr1" class="inline-fig internalNav"></button><figcaption><div class="hlFld-FigureCaption caption"><p class="first last">Figure 1. (a) The illustration for the interface engineering. (b) The atomic configurations of bulk phase of Na<sub>2</sub>O, Na<sub>2</sub>S, and Na<sub>2</sub>Se. (c) The diffusion energy barrier of Na atom in the bulk phases. (d) The optimized structures of the interface of Na<sub>2</sub>O/Na<sub>2</sub>Se, Na<sub>2</sub>O/Na<sub>2</sub>S, and Na<sub>2</sub>S/Na<sub>2</sub>Se.</p></div></figcaption><div class="figure-bottom-links"><div class="download-hi-res-img"><a title="High Resolution Image" href="/cms/10.1021/acs.nanolett.0c02595/asset/images/large/nl0c02595_0001.jpeg"><span>High Resolution Image</span></a></div><div class="download-ppt"><a title="Download MS PowerPoint Slide" href="/action/downloadFigures?doi=10.1021/acs.nanolett.0c02595&amp;id=fig1"><span>Download MS PowerPoint Slide</span></a></div></div></figure><div class="NLM_p">Then, three interface models of the Na<sub>2</sub>O/Na<sub>2</sub>S, Na<sub>2</sub>O/Na<sub>2</sub>Se, and Na<sub>2</sub>S/Na<sub>2</sub>Se were fabricated to investigate the diffusion behavior of Na ion in the interface phases. As shown in <a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig1" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure </a><a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig1" id="" class=" internalNav">1</a>d, the optimized structures of the interface phases show that the Na<sub>2</sub>S/Na<sub>2</sub>Se encounters the smallest structural distortion at the contact area of the interface while both the Na<sub>2</sub>O/Na<sub>2</sub>S and Na<sub>2</sub>O/Na<sub>2</sub>Se junctions exhibit partial aggregation for the Na atom. <a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig2" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure </a><a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig2" id="" class=" internalNav">2</a>a shows that the interface binding energy <i>E</i><sub>ib</sub> of the Na<sub>2</sub>S/Na<sub>2</sub>Se, Na<sub>2</sub>O–Na<sub>2</sub>S, and Na<sub>2</sub>O–Na<sub>2</sub>Se interfaces is 24, 10, and 13 meV/Å<sup>2</sup><sub>,</sub> respectively, where the Na<sub>2</sub>S/Na<sub>2</sub>Se interface structure possesses the strongest interface strength. As shown in <a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig2" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure </a><a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig2" id="" class=" internalNav">2</a>b and <a href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/acs.nanolett.0c02595/suppl_file/nl0c02595_si_001.pdf" class="ext-link">Figures S1–S3</a>, the in-planar average charge density exhibits that there exists electron exchange in the interface contact area. The two-dimensional cross-sectional view of charge density differences indicates that interface combination via bond formation occurs between the outmost Na metal atom of one phase and nonmetal elements (O, S, Se) of the other phase. <a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig2" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure </a><a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig2" id="" class=" internalNav">2</a>c shows that the <i>s</i> orbital of the surface Na<sub>1</sub> atom (belonging to the Na<sub>2</sub>Se outer surface) in the interface simultaneously couples with the wide packet dispersed by the <i>p</i> orbital of S and Se elements in the range from −1.8 to −0.7 eV, while the Na<sub>2</sub> atom (belonging to the Na<sub>2</sub>S outer surface) exhibits a similar bonding behavior to the S and Se elements in the interfacial area. These results can clearly describe the situation when the interface is formed between the two phases.</div><figure id="fig2" data-index="2" class="article__inlineFigure"><h2 class="fig-label">Figure 2</h2><button class="figure-viewer__trigger" aria-label="open figure viewer"><img src="/cms/10.1021/acs.nanolett.0c02595/asset/images/medium/nl0c02595_0002.gif" alt="" data-lg-src="/cms/10.1021/acs.nanolett.0c02595/asset/images/large/nl0c02595_0002.jpeg" id="gr2" class="inline-fig internalNav"></button><figcaption><div class="hlFld-FigureCaption caption"><p class="first last">Figure 2. (a) Interface binding energy of three interface structures. (b) Charge density difference and the related two-dimensional cross-sectional view of Na<sub>2</sub>S/Na<sub>2</sub>Se interface structures. (c) Partial density of states of the selected atoms (Na, S, Se) in the contact area of the Na<sub>2</sub>S/Na<sub>2</sub>Se interface. (d) Diffusion energy barrier of the Na atom in three interface structures. (e) XRD pattern of the SnS@3DC and SnS-SnSe<sub>2</sub>@3DC composites. XPS spectrum of (f) Sn 3d in the SnS-SnSe<sub>2</sub>@3DC composite.</p></div></figcaption><div class="figure-bottom-links"><div class="download-hi-res-img"><a title="High Resolution Image" href="/cms/10.1021/acs.nanolett.0c02595/asset/images/large/nl0c02595_0002.jpeg"><span>High Resolution Image</span></a></div><div class="download-ppt"><a title="Download MS PowerPoint Slide" href="/action/downloadFigures?doi=10.1021/acs.nanolett.0c02595&amp;id=fig2"><span>Download MS PowerPoint Slide</span></a></div></div></figure><div class="NLM_p last">We then calculate the diffusion energy barrier of the Na atom in the Na<sub>2</sub>O/Na<sub>2</sub>S, Na<sub>2</sub>O/Na<sub>2</sub>Se, and Na<sub>2</sub>S/Na<sub>2</sub>Se interfaces. As shown in <a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig2" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure </a><a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig2" id="" class=" internalNav">2</a>d, the diffusion barriers of Na atom in the Na<sub>2</sub>O/Na<sub>2</sub>S, Na<sub>2</sub>O/Na<sub>2</sub>Se, and Na<sub>2</sub>S/Na<sub>2</sub>Se interfaces are 0.61, 0.68, and 0.39 eV, respectively. Hence, the Na<sub>2</sub>S/Na<sub>2</sub>Se interface exhibits the smallest diffusion energy barrier among the three interface structures. Moreover, this diffusion energy barrier of Na<sub>2</sub>S/Na<sub>2</sub>Se is even smaller than that in the bulk phase (Na<sub>2</sub>S and Na<sub>2</sub>Se) diffusion processes. To further explore the reason for the different diffusion behaviors of the Na atom in the three interface structures, electronic structure analysis was performed. It can be rationally predicted that the remarkable difference of the electronegativity between the O (3.61) and S (2.589)/Se (2.424) elements can make a great influence on the high diffusion barrier of Na ion in the Na<sub>2</sub>O/Na<sub>2</sub>S and Na<sub>2</sub>O/Na<sub>2</sub>Se interface structures. When the interface of Na<sub>2</sub>O/Na<sub>2</sub>S and Na<sub>2</sub>O/Na<sub>2</sub>Se formed, the surface Na atoms in the Na<sub>2</sub>S and Na<sub>2</sub>Se were attracted by the electrostatic effect of the O atom, which then makes the surface Na atom deviate from the original position and enlarges the bond length between the surface Na atoms and S/Se atom (<a href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/acs.nanolett.0c02595/suppl_file/nl0c02595_si_001.pdf" class="ext-link">Figure S4</a>). As a result, the surface Na atoms of the Na<sub>2</sub>S and Na<sub>2</sub>Se exhibit localized atom aggregation areas near the Na<sub>2</sub>O surface, which can be seen in <a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig1" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure </a><a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig1" id="" class=" internalNav">1</a>d and <a href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/acs.nanolett.0c02595/suppl_file/nl0c02595_si_001.pdf" class="ext-link">Figure S5</a>. The aggregation area of the surface Na atom compressed the volume zone of the interface Na atom, leading to a much higher diffusion barrier. Through the analysis of the diffusion path intermediate images (<a href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/acs.nanolett.0c02595/suppl_file/nl0c02595_si_001.pdf" class="ext-link">Figure S6</a>), we found that the efficient diffusivity of the Na atom in the Na<sub>2</sub>S/Na<sub>2</sub>Se junction occurs through knock-off mechanisms, which is caused by the well-ordered interface structures due to their similar electronegativity and similar lattice constant. In the knock-off mechanism, downward migration of ions at the high-energy sites can offset a portion of the energy barrier of the upward migration of ions at low-energy sites. <a href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref33" id="referenceLink" data-rid="ref33" aria-label="reference 33">(33)</a> This unique concerted diffusion can make the diffusion of the interface Na atom encounter a smaller diffusion barrier than in the other two interface systems. Such characteristics on high rate performance give foresight for the advantage of designing a good power density anode of SIBs by constructing metal sulfide/metal selenide composite materials.</div></div><div id="sec2_2" class="NLM_sec NLM_sec_level_2"><div id="ac_i5" class="anchor-spacer"></div><h3 class="article-section__title" id="_i5"> Experimental Validation</h3><div class="NLM_p">In order to verify the smooth diffusion of Na along the Na<sub>2</sub>S/Na<sub>2</sub>Se interface, a metal sulfide/metal selenide heterogeneous structure was designed and synthesized. First, SnS nanocrystals anchored on three-dimensional ultrathin carbon networks (SnS@3DC) were synthesized by a simple template-assisted calcination method. <a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig2" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure </a><a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig2" id="" class=" internalNav">2</a>e shows the X-ray diffraction (XRD) pattern of SnS@3DC composite. Evidently, all diffraction peaks can be well indexed to the orthorhombic SnS crystal structure. Scanning electron microscopy (SEM) and transmission electron microscopy (TEM) images of the SnS@3DC composite are shown as <a href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/acs.nanolett.0c02595/suppl_file/nl0c02595_si_001.pdf" class="ext-link">Figure S7</a>. In order to obtain the sulfide/selenide heterointerface structure, SnS@3DC composite was subjected to a chemical vapor deposition process under a gaseous selenium environment to partially selenize SnS. The XRD pattern in <a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig2" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure </a><a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig2" id="" class=" internalNav">2</a>e confirms that after selenization, SnSe<sub>2</sub> appears in addition to the diffraction peaks of SnS. SEM and TEM images of the SnS-SnSe<sub>2</sub>@3DC composite are shown in <a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig3" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure </a><a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig3" id="" class=" internalNav">3</a>a–f. It can be seen that nanocubes with a diameter of about 200 nm are generated on the carbon networks. The high-resolution SEM image of <a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig3" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure </a><a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig3" id="" class=" internalNav">3</a>d confirms that nanocubes and nanocrystals coexist and are interconnected. It is depicted from the TEM images in <a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig3" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure </a><a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig3" id="" class=" internalNav">3</a>e–f that the surface of the nanocubes is coated with SnS nanocrystals (exposed (021) crystal plane), while the inside is the SnSe<sub>2</sub> phase (exposed (101) crystal plane). <a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig3" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure </a><a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig3" id="" class=" internalNav">3</a>g shows the STEM elemental mapping of the SnS-SnSe<sub>2</sub>@3DC composite. It demonstrates that the composite is composed of C, Sn, S, and Se elements and that the monodispersed nanocrystals in the upper left corner are SnS while the flakes in the lower right corner are SnSe<sub>2</sub>. Meanwhile, there are also nanocrystals on the flake cubes (<a href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/acs.nanolett.0c02595/suppl_file/nl0c02595_si_001.pdf" class="ext-link">Figure S8</a>).</div><figure id="fig3" data-index="3" class="article__inlineFigure"><h2 class="fig-label">Figure 3</h2><button class="figure-viewer__trigger" aria-label="open figure viewer"><img src="/cms/10.1021/acs.nanolett.0c02595/asset/images/medium/nl0c02595_0003.gif" alt="" data-lg-src="/cms/10.1021/acs.nanolett.0c02595/asset/images/large/nl0c02595_0003.jpeg" id="gr3" class="inline-fig internalNav"></button><figcaption><div class="hlFld-FigureCaption caption"><p class="first last">Figure 3. (a–d) SEM images, (e, f) TEM images, and (g) STEM elemental mapping of the SnS-SnSe<sub>2</sub>@3DC composite.</p></div></figcaption><div class="figure-bottom-links"><div class="download-hi-res-img"><a title="High Resolution Image" href="/cms/10.1021/acs.nanolett.0c02595/asset/images/large/nl0c02595_0003.jpeg"><span>High Resolution Image</span></a></div><div class="download-ppt"><a title="Download MS PowerPoint Slide" href="/action/downloadFigures?doi=10.1021/acs.nanolett.0c02595&amp;id=fig3"><span>Download MS PowerPoint Slide</span></a></div></div></figure><div class="NLM_p">In order to investigate the surface electron state between the SnS/SnSe<sub>2</sub> interfaces, X-ray photoelectron spectroscopy (XPS) was employed on the SnS-SnSe<sub>2</sub>@3DC composite. First, Sn 3d (<a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig2" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure </a><a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig2" id="" class=" internalNav">2</a>f) can be divided to two pairs of peaks, in which the peaks at 494.9 and 486.4 eV can be assigned to Sn 3d<sub>3/2</sub> and Sn 3d<sub>5/2</sub> of Sn<sup>4+</sup> and the peaks at 494.3 and 485.8 eV are ascribed to Sn<sup>2+</sup>, confirming the formation of Sn<sup>4+</sup>/Sn<sup>2+</sup> heterostructures. <a href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref34" id="referenceLink" data-rid="ref34" aria-label="reference 34">(34)</a> Besides, Se 3d (<a href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/acs.nanolett.0c02595/suppl_file/nl0c02595_si_001.pdf" class="ext-link">Figure S9a</a>) can be divided into oxidized Se (58.0 eV) and reduced Se (55.1 and 54.2 eV), which correspond to Se<sup>4+</sup> and Se<sup>2–</sup>, respectively. <a href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref35" id="referenceLink" data-rid="ref35" aria-label="reference 35">(35)</a> The oxidized Se may be due to the surface oxidation of Se adsorbed on the carbon network in the air. As for the S 2p, two pairs of peaks can be detected (<a href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/acs.nanolett.0c02595/suppl_file/nl0c02595_si_001.pdf" class="ext-link">Figure S9b</a>). The peaks at 166.5 and 164.3 eV relate to the S<sup>0</sup> bonding with C, while the peaks at 162.8 and 160.7 eV are contributes to S<sup>2–</sup>. <a href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref36" id="referenceLink" data-rid="ref36" aria-label="reference 36">(36)</a> The above results confirm the electron transfer and bonding state between S–Sn–Se, which would enhance the ionic-transfer kinetics in electrochemical reactions. Hence, the metal sulfide/metal selenide heterogeneous structure is preliminary achieved.</div><div class="NLM_p"><a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig4" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure </a><a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig4" id="" class=" internalNav">4</a>a and b displays the initial three cyclic voltammetry (CV) curves of the SnS@3DC and SnS-SnSe<sub>2</sub>@3DC electrodes at 0.2 mV s<sup>–1</sup>. The first scan is different as follows. The wide cathodic peaks in the first scan at about 1.2 V in both electrodes correspond to the formation of solid electrolyte interphase (SEI) film, and its disappearance in the subsequent cycles proves its stability throughout the electrochemical reactions. From the second cycle, the curves almost overlap, demonstrating a highly reversible reaction. For the SnS@3DC electrode, a pair of redox peaks between 1 and 1.5 V correspond to the conversion reaction between SnS and Sn. Besides this, two pairs of peaks between 0.01 and 0.8 V correspond to the multistep alloying/dealloying reaction of Sn and Na–Sn alloy. <a href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref37 ref38" id="referenceLink" data-rid="ref37 ref38" aria-label="reference 37 and 38">(37,38)</a> While for the SnS-SnSe<sub>2</sub>@3DC electrode, in addition to the sodiation/desodiation of SnS, redox peaks at high voltage appear (1.5–2.5 V), which are ascribed to the conversion reaction between SnSe<sub>2</sub> and Sn. <a href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref39" id="referenceLink" data-rid="ref39" aria-label="reference 39">(39)</a> Notably, the cathodic peaks at about 2 V, which represents the sodiation of SnSe<sub>2</sub>, are highly reversible, proving an excellent electrochemical stability even though the particle size is relatively large. <a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig4" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure </a><a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig4" id="" class=" internalNav">4</a>c and d show the cycle and rate performance of the SnS@3DC and SnS-SnSe<sub>2</sub>@3DC electrodes. The SnS-SnSe<sub>2</sub>@3DC electrode delivers initial charge and discharge capacities of 624.9 and 1131.3 mAh g<sup>–1</sup> at 0.1 A g<sup>–1</sup>. The huge capacity loss can be ascribed to the irreversible formation of SEI film. After a slight decay, the capacity stabilizes at about 580 mAh g<sup>–1</sup> and maintains for 100 cycles with a capacity retention of 90.2%, which is much higher than that of the SnS@3DC electrode (about 480 mAh g<sup>–1</sup> with a capacity retention of 78%). More importantly, the slow kinetic process in the conversion reaction usually results in low columbic efficiency and cycle stability. <a href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref34 ref35 ref36" id="referenceLink" data-rid="ref34 ref35 ref36" aria-label="reference 34 to 36">(34−36)</a> The columbic efficiency of the SnS@3DC electrode even after 50 cycles is only about 98%, while enhanced ionic diffusion behavior increases the columbic efficiency of SnS-SnSe<sub>2</sub>@3DC electrode to about 99%. For the rate performance, the SnS-SnSe<sub>2</sub>@3DC electrode delivers average capacities of 529, 471, 420, 387, 328, and 267 mAh g<sup>–1</sup> at current densities of 0.2, 0.5, 1, 2, 5, and 10 A g<sup>–1</sup>, respectively. The normalized capacity at 10 A g<sup>–1</sup> compared to 0.1 A g<sup>–1</sup> is 45.6%, which is much high than that of the SnS@3DC electrode (35.5%), exhibiting a better rate capability.</div><figure id="fig4" data-index="4" class="article__inlineFigure"><h2 class="fig-label">Figure 4</h2><button class="figure-viewer__trigger" aria-label="open figure viewer"><img src="/cms/10.1021/acs.nanolett.0c02595/asset/images/medium/nl0c02595_0004.gif" alt="" data-lg-src="/cms/10.1021/acs.nanolett.0c02595/asset/images/large/nl0c02595_0004.jpeg" id="gr4" class="inline-fig internalNav"></button><figcaption><div class="hlFld-FigureCaption caption"><p class="first last">Figure 4. (a, b) CV curves, (c) cycle performance, and (d) rate performance of the SnS@3DC and SnS-SnSe<sub>2</sub>@3DC electrodes. (e) The <i>b</i>-values calculated through a cathodic scan and (f) the capacitive-controlled contribution at different scan rates of the SnS-SnSe<sub>2</sub>@3DC electrode. (g) EIS curves at various voltages of the SnS@3DC and SnS-SnSe<sub>2</sub>@3DC electrodes. (h) Relationship between −Zim and ω<sup>–1/2</sup>.(i) Diffusion coefficients calculated at various voltages for the SnS-SnSe<sub>2</sub>@3DC electrode.</p></div></figcaption><div class="figure-bottom-links"><div class="download-hi-res-img"><a title="High Resolution Image" href="/cms/10.1021/acs.nanolett.0c02595/asset/images/large/nl0c02595_0004.jpeg"><span>High Resolution Image</span></a></div><div class="download-ppt"><a title="Download MS PowerPoint Slide" href="/action/downloadFigures?doi=10.1021/acs.nanolett.0c02595&amp;id=fig4"><span>Download MS PowerPoint Slide</span></a></div></div></figure><div class="NLM_p">To reveal the energy storage mechanism in depth, <a href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/acs.nanolett.0c02595/suppl_file/nl0c02595_si_001.pdf" class="ext-link">Figure S10a and b</a> compares the CV curves of SnS@3DC and SnS-SnSe<sub>2</sub>@3DC electrodes at scan rates of 0.2–1.0 mV s<sup>–1</sup>. As the scan rate increases, the redox peaks become stronger and are accompanied by a shift in peak position, indicating a slight polarization phenomenon. Capacitive- and diffusion-controlled electrochemical reactions can be differentiated from the power-law relationship between current (<i>i</i>) and scanning rate (<i>v</i>). <a href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref40 ref41" id="referenceLink" data-rid="ref40 ref41" aria-label="reference 40 and 41">(40,41)</a><span class="NLM_disp-formula"><div class="MathJaxEquation" id="eq1"><div class="equation"><img class="imgWithoutFigViewer " src="/cms/10.1021/acs.nanolett.0c02595/asset/images/medium/nl0c02595_m001.gif" alt="" id="_i6"></div><div class="label"><span class="labelSpan">(1)</span></div></div></span>Whereas <i>b</i> = 0.5 represents the diffusion-controlled process, <i>b</i> = 1 represents the capacitive-controlled process. According to the cathodic scan, <i>b</i> values calculated by <a class="ref internalNav" href="#eq1" aria-label="eq 1">eq </a><a class="ref internalNav" href="#eq1" aria-label="1">1</a> are shown in <a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig4" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure </a><a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig4" id="" class=" internalNav">4</a>e. It is worth noting that the <i>b</i> values are always greater than 0.75 throughout the discharge process. Moreover, among the occurrence of the two main conversion reactions, the <i>b</i> values approach 1, which proves that the sodiation of SnS and SnSe<sub>2</sub> are both capacitive-controlled processes. The right hand side, <i>av</i><sup><i>b</i></sup>, of <a class="ref internalNav" href="#eq1" aria-label="eq 1">eq </a><a class="ref internalNav" href="#eq1" aria-label="1">1</a> can be divided to two parts:<span class="NLM_disp-formula"><div class="MathJaxEquation" id="eq2"><div class="equation"><img class="imgWithoutFigViewer " src="/cms/10.1021/acs.nanolett.0c02595/asset/images/medium/nl0c02595_m002.gif" alt="" id="_i7"></div><div class="label"><span class="labelSpan">(2)</span></div></div></span>where the former represents <i>b</i> = 1 and the latter represents <i>b</i> = 0.5. Therefore, the proportion of <i>k</i><sub>1</sub><i>v</i> is the contribution of capacitive-controlled energy storage in the capacity. As depicted in <a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig4" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure </a><a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig4" id="" class=" internalNav">4</a>f, it contributes 67.7% of the capacity at a low scan rate of 0.2 mV s<sup>–1</sup>. However, as the scan rate increases, this ratio gradually increases, reaching 82.3% at 1.0 mV s<sup>–1</sup> which is much higher than that of the SnS@3DC electrode (see <a href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/acs.nanolett.0c02595/suppl_file/nl0c02595_si_001.pdf" class="ext-link">Figure S10c and d</a>). In addition, from the shape of redox peaks, both of the conversion reactions of SnS and SnSe<sub>2</sub> in the range of 1–2 V can contribute a lot of capacitive charge storage.</div><div class="NLM_p last"><a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig4" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure </a><a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig4" id="" class=" internalNav">4</a>g shows the ex-situ electrochemical impedance spectroscopy (EIS) curves of the SnS@3DC and SnS-SnSe<sub>2</sub>@3DC electrodes at different charging states from 0.01 to 1.5 V. These curves are composed of a semicircle at high frequency and a sloping line at low frequency. The radius of the former represents the SEI and charge-transfer resistance, while the slope of the latter is related to the ionic diffusion in the bulk phase of the electrodes. It can be seen that the internal resistances of the two electrodes are gradually reduced as the Na<sup>+</sup> extraction takes place, while the resistance of the SnS-SnSe<sub>2</sub>@3DC electrode is smaller than that of SnS@3DC, indicating its smoother electrode reactions in dynamics. According to the low-frequency sloping line, the calculation formula of Na<sup>+</sup> diffusion coefficient is as follows:<span class="NLM_disp-formula"><div class="MathJaxEquation" id="eq3"><div class="equation"><img class="imgWithoutFigViewer " src="/cms/10.1021/acs.nanolett.0c02595/asset/images/medium/nl0c02595_m003.gif" alt="" id="_i8"></div><div class="label"><span class="labelSpan">(3)</span></div></div></span><span class="NLM_disp-formula"><div class="MathJaxEquation" id="eq4"><div class="equation"><img class="imgWithoutFigViewer " src="/cms/10.1021/acs.nanolett.0c02595/asset/images/medium/nl0c02595_m004.gif" alt="" id="_i9"></div><div class="label"><span class="labelSpan">(4)</span></div></div></span>The <i>R</i> (gas constant), <i>T</i> (absolute temperature), <i>n</i> (charge-transfer number), <i>F</i> (Faradaic constant), <i>A</i> (surface area), and <i>C</i> in <a class="ref internalNav" href="#eq3" aria-label="eq 3">eq </a><a class="ref internalNav" href="#eq3" aria-label="3">3</a> for the two electrodes are almost the same, so the <i>D</i> (diffusion coefficient) is inversely proportional to σ<sub arrange="stagger">w</sub><sup arrange="stagger">2</sup>. Further, σ<sub>w</sub> is the slope of <i>Z</i>′ (impedance) to ω<sup>–1/2</sup>, and the calculation results are shown as <a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig4" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure </a><a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig4" id="" class=" internalNav">4</a>h and <a href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/acs.nanolett.0c02595/suppl_file/nl0c02595_si_001.pdf" class="ext-link">Figure S11</a>. <a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig4" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure </a><a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig4" id="" class=" internalNav">4</a>i depicts the final values of σ<sub arrange="stagger">w</sub><sup arrange="stagger">2</sup> during the charging process, which evidently represents the diffusion coefficients of the two electrodes. Combined with CV curves, the dealloying reaction of Sn–Na alloy at low voltage has a higher ion diffusion coefficient while the conversion reaction of SnS and SnSe<sub>2</sub> at high voltage is slow in its kinetics. However, through the design of the SnS–SnSe<sub>2</sub> structure, the ion diffusion coefficient can be increased by about 1.5 times, so as to obtain better electrochemical performance.</div></div></div><div id="sec3" class="NLM_sec NLM_sec_level_1"><div class="article_content-header"><div class="article_content-header-row"><div class="article_content-title" tabindex="0"><h2 id="_i10">Conclusion</h2><div class="article__copy"><button class="article__copy__btn"><i class="article__copy__icon icon-copy-solid" aria-hidden="true"></i></button><span class="article__copy__text">Click to copy section link</span><span class="article__copy__success">Section link copied!</span></div></div></div></div><hr><div class="NLM_p last">In conclusion, we demonstrated that the Na<sub>2</sub>S/Na<sub>2</sub>Se heterostructure possesses the fastest diffusion channel of Na ions among the Na<sub>2</sub>S/Na<sub>2</sub>O, Na<sub>2</sub>O/Na<sub>2</sub>Se, and Na<sub>2</sub>S/Na<sub>2</sub>Se materials by combining first-principle calculations and experimental verification. Electron analysis revealed that the good diffusion behavior in the Na<sub>2</sub>S/Na<sub>2</sub>Se heterointerface is induced by the small interface deformation, similar electronegativity (S and Se), and similar lattice constants. Based on this simulation result, we fabricated a reasonable design for the SnS/SnSe<sub>2</sub>@3DC heterojunction as the anode of the SIBs to verify the theoretical results. The results show that the SnS/SnSe<sub>2</sub>@3DC electrode exhibits a high columbic efficiency (up to 99%) and superior rate performance (529 mAh g<sup>–1</sup> at 0.2 A g<sup>–1</sup>, 328 mAh g<sup>–1</sup> at 5 A g<sup>–1</sup>, and 267 mAh g<sup>–1</sup> at 10 A g<sup>–1</sup>). Our work revealed the diffusion behavior of Na in the heterostructure electrode after the initial sodiation process and shed a new light on exploring the superior rate performance anode for SIBs.</div></div><div class="NLM_back"><div class="article_supporting-info" id="notes1"><div class="article_content-header"><div class="article_content-header-row"><div class="article_content-title" tabindex="0"><h2 id="_i11">Supporting Information</h2><div class="article__copy"><button class="article__copy__btn"><i class="article__copy__icon icon-copy-solid" aria-hidden="true"></i></button><span class="article__copy__text">Click to copy section link</span><span class="article__copy__success">Section link copied!</span></div></div></div></div><hr><p class="last">The Supporting Information is available free of charge at <a href="/doi/10.1021/acs.nanolett.0c02595?goto=supporting-info" class="ext-link">https://pubs.acs.org/doi/10.1021/acs.nanolett.0c02595</a>.</p><ul class="NLM_list-list_type-label" id="silist"><li><p class="inline">Computational methods and experimental details (<a href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/acs.nanolett.0c02595/suppl_file/nl0c02595_si_001.pdf" class="ext-link">PDF</a>)</p></li></ul><p></p><div class="supInfoBoxOnFTP "><div class="sup-info-attachments"><ul><li><a class="suppl-anchor" href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/acs.nanolett.0c02595/suppl_file/nl0c02595_si_001.pdf">nl0c02595_si_001.pdf (1.87 MB)</a></li></ul></div></div></div><div class="testing" data-doi="10.1021/acs.nanolett.0c02595" data-url="https://widgets.figshare.com/static/figshare.js" id="figshare-widget" style="display: block;"></div><div class="TermsAndConditionsDropzone3" data-pb-dropzone="TermsAndConditionsDropzone3"><div data-widget-def="UX3HTMLWidget" data-widget-id="85b54935-30e9-4058-a6d2-0b40fb754a85" class="mb-4">
