M.</span></span> <span> </span><span class="NLM_article-title">Nano-sized transition-metal oxides as negative-electrode materials for lithium-ion batteries</span>. <i>Nature</i>  <span class="NLM_year" style="font-weight: bold;">2000</span>,  <span class="NLM_volume"><i>407</i></span>,  <span class="NLM_fpage">496</span>– <span class="NLM_lpage">499</span>, <span class="refDoi">&nbsp;DOI:
                10.1038/35035045</span> </span><div class=" links-group getFTR"><div class="getFTR__content getFTR__placeholder"></div><span class="references__suffix"><a class="google-scholar" href="/action/getFTRLinkout?url=http%3A%2F%2Fscholar.google.com%2Fscholar_lookup%3Fhl%3Den%26volume%3D407%26publication_year%3D2000%26pages%3D496-499%26journal%3DNature%26author%3DP.%2BPoizot%26author%3DS.%2BLaruelle%26author%3DS.%2BGrugeon%26author%3DL.%2BDupont%26author%3DJ.%2BM.%2BTarascon%26title%3DNano-sized%2Btransition-metal%2Boxides%2Bas%2Bnegative-electrode%2Bmaterials%2Bfor%2Blithium-ion%2Bbatteries%26doi%3D10.1038%252F35035045&amp;doi=10.1021%2Facs.nanolett.0c02595&amp;doiOfLink=10.1038%2F35035045&amp;linkType=gs&amp;linkLocation=Reference&amp;linkSource=FULL_TEXT" target="_blank">Google Scholar</a></span><div class="casRecord" id="
                        cas_cit1R"><div class="casContent"><span class="casTitleNuber">1</span><div class="casTitle"><span class="NLM_cas:atitle">Nano-sized transition-metal oxides as negative-electrode materials for lithium-ion batteries</span></div><div class="casAuthors">Poizot P; Laruelle S; Grugeon S; Dupont L; Tarascon J M</div><div class="citationInfo"><span class="NLM_cas:title">Nature</span>
        (<span class="NLM_cas:date">2000</span>),
    <span class="NLM_cas:volume">407</span>
        (<span class="NLM_cas:issue">6803</span>),
    <span class="NLM_cas:pages">496-9</span>
        ISSN:<span class="NLM_cas:issn">0028-0836</span>.
    </div><div class="casAbstract">Rechargeable solid-state batteries have long been considered an attractive power source for a wide variety of applications, and in particular, lithium-ion batteries are emerging as the technology of choice for portable electronics.  One of the main challenges in the design of these batteries is to ensure that the electrodes maintain their integrity over many discharge-recharge cycles.  Although promising electrode systems have recently been proposed, their lifespans are limited by Li-alloying agglomeration or the growth of passivation layers, which prevent the fully reversible insertion of Li ions into the negative electrodes.  Here we report that electrodes made of nanoparticles of transition-metal oxides (MO, where M is Co, Ni, Cu or Fe) demonstrate electrochemical capacities of 700 mA h g(-1), with 100% capacity retention for up to 100 cycles and high recharging rates.  The mechanism of Li reactivity differs from the classical Li insertion/deinsertion or Li-alloying processes, and involves the formation and decomposition of Li2O, accompanying the reduction and oxidation of metal nanoparticles (in the range 1-5 nanometres) respectively.  We expect that the use of transition-metal nanoparticles to enhance surface electrochemical reactivity will lead to further improvements in the performance of lithium-ion batteries.</div></div></div></div></div></div></li><li id="ref2"><div class="reference"><strong class="refLabel"><a href="javascript:void(0);" data-citation="" data-rid="ref2" class="refNumLink">2</a></strong><div class="NLM_citation references__item" id="cit2" data-doi="10.1021/nl800957b"><span><span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Yoo, E. J.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Kim, J.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Hosono, E.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Zhou, H. S.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Kudo, T.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Honma, I.</span></span> <span> </span><span class="NLM_article-title">Large reversible Li storage of graphene nanosheet families for use in rechargeable lithium ion batteries</span>. <i>Nano Lett.</i>  <span class="NLM_year" style="font-weight: bold;">2008</span>,  <span class="NLM_volume"><i>8</i></span> (<span class="NLM_issue">8</span>),  <span class="NLM_fpage">2277</span>– <span class="NLM_lpage">2282</span>, <span class="refDoi">&nbsp;DOI:
                10.1021/nl800957b</span> </span><div class=" links-group getFTR"><div class="getFTR__content getFTR__placeholder"></div><span class="references__suffix"><a class="google-scholar" href="/action/getFTRLinkout?url=http%3A%2F%2Fscholar.google.com%2Fscholar_lookup%3Fhl%3Den%26volume%3D8%26publication_year%3D2008%26pages%3D2277-2282%26journal%3DNano%2BLett.%26issue%3D8%26author%3DE.%2BJ.%2BYoo%26author%3DJ.%2BKim%26author%3DE.%2BHosono%26author%3DH.%2BS.%2BZhou%26author%3DT.%2BKudo%26author%3DI.%2BHonma%26title%3DLarge%2Breversible%2BLi%2Bstorage%2Bof%2Bgraphene%2Bnanosheet%2Bfamilies%2Bfor%2Buse%2Bin%2Brechargeable%2Blithium%2Bion%2Bbatteries%26doi%3D10.1021%252Fnl800957b&amp;doi=10.1021%2Facs.nanolett.0c02595&amp;doiOfLink=10.1021%2Fnl800957b&amp;linkType=gs&amp;linkLocation=Reference&amp;linkSource=FULL_TEXT" target="_blank">Google Scholar</a></span><div class="casRecord" id="
                        cas_cit2R"><div class="casContent"><span class="casTitleNuber">2</span><div class="casTitle"><span class="NLM_cas:atitle">Large Reversible Li Storage of Graphene Nanosheet Families for Use in Rechargeable Lithium Ion Batteries</span></div><div class="casAuthors">Yoo, EunJoo; Kim, Jedeok; Hosono, Eiji; Zhou, Hao-shen; Kudo, Tetsuichi; Honma, Itaru</div><div class="citationInfo"><span class="NLM_cas:title">Nano Letters</span>
        (<span class="NLM_cas:date">2008</span>),

        </div><div class="casAbstract">The lithium storage properties of graphene nanosheet materials as high capacity anode materials for lithium secondary batteries were investigated.  Graphite is a practical anode material used for lithium secondary batteries, because of its capability for reversible lithium ion intercalation in the layered crystals, and the structural similarities of graphene nanosheet to graphite may provide another type of intercalation anode compd.  While the accommodation of lithium in these layered compds. is influenced by the layer spacing between the graphene nanosheets, control of the intergraphene sheet distance through interacting mols. such as carbon nanotubes or fullerenes (C60) might be crucial for enhancement of the storage capacity.         </div><div class="casAbstract">Li (Na)-metal batteries are the most promising batteries for next-generation elec. energy storage due to their high volumetric energy d. and gravimetric energy d.  However, their applications have been prevented by uncontrollable dendrite growth and large vol. expansion during the stripping/plating process.  To address this issue, the key strategy is to realize uniform Li (Na) deposition during the stripping/plating process.  Here, a thin lithiophilic layer consisting of RuO2 particles anchored on brush-like 3D carbon cloth (RuO2@CC) is prepd. by a simple soln.-based method.  After infusion of Li, the RuO2@CC transfers to Li-Ru@CC.  Ru nanoparticles not only play a role in leading Li+ (Na+) to plate on the 3D carbon framework, but also lower local c.d. because of the good elec. cond.  D. functional theory calcns. demonstrate that Ru metal, the reaction product of alkali metal and Ru, can lead Li+ to plate evenly around C fiber owing to the strong binding energy with Li+.  The Li-Ru@CC anode shows ultralong cycle life (1500 h at 5 mA/cm2).  The full cell of Li-Ru@CC|LiFePO4 exhibits lower polarization (90% capacity retention after 650 cycles).  Na metal batteries based on Na-Ru@CC anodes can achieve similar improvement.</div></div></div></div></div></div></li><li id="ref5"><div class="reference"><strong class="refLabel"><a href="javascript:void(0);" data-citation="" data-rid="ref5" class="refNumLink">5</a></strong><div class="NLM_citation references__item" id="cit5" data-doi="10.1021/ja025282c"><span><span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Kerr, J.     </div><div class="casAbstract">The use of graphene as a conductive additive to enhance the discharge capacity and rate capability of LiNi(1/3)Co(1/3)Mn(1/3)O(2) electrode material has been demonstrated.  LiNi(1/3)Co(1/3)Mn(1/3)O(2) and its composite with graphene (90:10 wt %) were prepared by microemulsion and ball-milling techniques, respectively.  The structural and morphological features of the prepared materials were investigated with powder X-ray diffraction, scanning electron microscopy, transmission electron microscopy, Raman spectroscopy, and X-ray photoelectron spectroscopy.  Characterization techniques depict single-phase LiNi(1/3)Co(1/3)Mn(1/3)O(2) with particle sizes in the range of 220-280 nm.  Electrochemical studies on LiNi(1/3)Co(1/3)Mn(1/3)O(2) and LiNi(1/3)Co(1/3)Mn(1/3)O(2)-graphene were conducted using cyclic voltammetry, galvanostatic charge-discharge, and electrochemical impedance spectroscopy methods by constructing a lithium half-cell.  Cyclic voltammograms show the well-defined redox peaks corresponding to Ni(2+)/Ni(4+).  Charge-discharge tests were performed at different C rates: 0.05, 1, and 5 between 2.5 and 4.4 V.  The results indicate the better electrochemical performance of the LiNi(1/3)Co(1/3)Mn(1/3)O(2)-graphene composite in terms of high discharge capacity (188 mAh/g), good rate capability, and good cycling performance compared to LiNi(1/3)Mn(1/3)Co(1/3)O(2).                         cas_cit8R"><div class="casContent"><span class="casTitleNuber">8</span><div class="casTitle"><span class="NLM_cas:atitle">Transition metal dichalcogenides for alkali metal ion batteries: engineering strategies at the atomic level</span></div><div class="casAuthors">Chen, Biao; Chao, Dongliang; Liu, Enzuo; Jaroniec, Mietek; Zhao, Naiqin; Qiao, Shi-Zhang</div><div class="citationInfo"><span class="NLM_cas:title">Energy &amp; Environmental Science</span>
        (<span class="NLM_cas:date">2020</span>),
    <span class="NLM_cas:volume">13</span>
        (<span class="NLM_cas:issue">4</span>),
    <span class="NLM_cas:pages">1096-1131</span>CODEN:
                <span class="NLM_cas:coden">EESNBY</span>;
        ISSN:<span class="NLM_cas:issn">1754-5706</span>.
    
            (<span class="NLM_cas:orgname">Royal Society of Chemistry</span>)
        </div><div class="casAbstract">A review.  In the past few decades, great effort has been made toward the prepn. and development of advanced transition metal dichalcogenide (TMD) materials for anodes of alkali metal ion batteries (AMIBs).  However, their electrochem. performance is still severely impaired by structural aggregation and fracture during the conversion reaction.  To address these issues, various methodologies for the fabrication of hierarchical and hybrid nanostructures, with optimization of materials and electrodes, have been fully investigated and reviewed.  As regards tuning the TMD-based materials, extensive efforts have been undertaken toward optimization of their intrinsic structure at the at. level, including surface defects, interlayer spacing expansion, phase control, alloying, and heteroatom doping.  However, the design strategies and methods to manipulate the intrinsic structures and electrochem. mechanisms in AMIBs have not been fully summarized.  This review provides a well-timed and crit. appraisal of recent advances in the engineering of TMDs at the at. level for AMIBs, by combining computational and exptl. approaches.  The correlation between these strategies and electrochem. performance is highlighted.  The challenges and opportunities in this research field are also outlined.  We expect that this review would be beneficial for improving the overall knowledge on the charge storage mechanisms in TMDs and for pointing out the importance of intrinsic structure engineering for enhancing the performance of TMDs in energy storage.</div></div></div></div></div></div></li><li id="ref9"><div class="reference"><strong class="refLabel"><a href="javascript:void(0);" data-citation="" data-rid="ref9" class="refNumLink">9</a></strong><div class="NLM_citation references__item" id="cit9" data-doi="10.1002/aenm.201702998"><span><span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Bai, Q.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Yang, L.                         cas_cit11R"><div class="casContent"><span class="casTitleNuber">11</span><div class="casTitle"><span class="NLM_cas:atitle">Room-temperature stationary sodium-ion batteries for large-scale electric energy storage</span></div><div class="casAuthors">Pan, Huilin; Hu, Yong-Sheng; Chen, Liquan</div><div class="citationInfo"><span class="NLM_cas:title">Energy &amp; Environmental Science</span>
        (<span class="NLM_cas:date">2013</span>),
    <span class="NLM_cas:volume">6</span>
        (<span class="NLM_cas:issue">8</span>),
    <span class="NLM_cas:pages">2338-2360</span>CODEN:
                <span class="NLM_cas:coden">EESNBY</span>;

            (<span class="NLM_cas:orgname">Royal Society of Chemistry</span>)
        </div><div class="casAbstract">A review.  Room-temp. stationary sodium-ion batteries have attracted great attention particularly in large-scale elec. energy storage applications for renewable energy and smart grid because of the huge abundant sodium resources and low cost.  In this article, a variety of electrode materials including cathodes and anodes as well as electrolytes for room-temp. stationary sodium-ion batteries are briefly reviewed.  We compare the difference in storage behavior between Na and Li in their analogous electrodes and summarize the sodium storage mechanisms in the available electrode materials.  This review also includes some new results from our group and our thoughts on developing new materials.  Some perspectives and directions on designing better materials for practical applications are pointed out based on knowledge from the literature and our experience.  Through this extensive literature review, the search for suitable electrode and electrolyte materials for stationary sodium-ion batteries is still challenging.  However, after intensive research efforts, we believe that low-cost, long-life and room-temp. sodium-ion batteries would be promising for applications in large-scale energy storage system in the near future.</div></div></div></div></div></div></li><li id="ref12"><div class="reference"><strong class="refLabel"><a href="javascript:void(0);" data-citation="" data-rid="ref12" class="refNumLink">12</a></strong><div class="NLM_citation references__item" id="cit12" data-doi="10.1039/C6TA07425A"><span><span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Zhou, J.                         cas_cit12R"><div class="casContent"><span class="casTitleNuber">12</span><div class="casTitle"><span class="NLM_cas:atitle">Scalable synthesis of high-quality transition metal dichalcogenide nanosheets and their application as sodium-ion battery anodes</span></div><div class="casAuthors">Zhou, Jingwen; Qin, Jian; Guo, Lichao; Zhao, Naiqin; Shi, Chunsheng; Liu, En-zuo; He, Fang; Ma, Liying; Li, Jiajun; He, Chunnian</div><div class="citationInfo"><span class="NLM_cas:title">Journal of Materials Chemistry A: Materials for Energy and Sustainability</span>
        (<span class="NLM_cas:date">2016</span>),
    <span class="NLM_cas:volume">4</span>
        (<span class="NLM_cas:issue">44</span>),
    <span class="NLM_cas:pages">17370-17380</span>CODEN:
                <span class="NLM_cas:coden">JMCAET</span>;
        ISSN:<span class="NLM_cas:issn">2050-7496</span>.
    
            (<span class="NLM_cas:orgname">Royal Society of Chemistry</span>)
        </div><div class="casAbstract">Layered transition metal dichalcogenides (TMDs), such as MoS2, WS2, MoSe2, WSe2, etc., have received considerable interest recently due to their unique properties and fascinating applications.  In this work, we proposed a NaCl template-assisted in situ chem. vapor deposition (CVD) strategy for synthesizing high-quality two-dimensional (2D) WS2, MoS2, MoSe2 and WSe2 nanosheets with a single-cryst. structure on a large scale.  During the synthesis, 3D self-assembly of cubic NaCl particles can not only provide smooth surfaces to support the lateral growth, but also create a 2D confined space to restrict the thickening of TMD nanosheets, while the high-temp. annealing during the CVD process ensures high crystallinity and structural stability as well as remarkably promoting the cond. of the TMD nanosheets.  When evaluated as sodium-ion battery anodes, the representative WS2 nanosheets exhibited a high reversible specific capacity of 453 mA h g-1 at a c.d. of 0.1 A g-1, an excellent cycling performance and a greatly enhanced rate capability.  The charge storage mechanism together with the microstructure evolution of WS2 nanosheets during the sodiation/disodiation processes is responsible for their superior sodium storage performance.</div></div></div></div></div></div></li><li id="ref13"><div class="reference"><strong class="refLabel"><a href="javascript:void(0);" data-citation="" data-rid="ref13" class="refNumLink">13</a></strong><div class="NLM_citation references__item" id="cit13" data-doi="10.1002/ange.201209689"><span><span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Qian, J.                 10.1021/acs.jpcc.7b04642</span> </span><div class=" links-group getFTR"><div class="getFTR__content getFTR__placeholder"></div><span class="references__suffix"><a class="google-scholar" href="/action/getFTRLinkout?url=http%3A%2F%2Fscholar.google.com%2Fscholar_lookup%3Fhl%3Den%26volume%3D121%26publication_year%3D2017%26pages%3D19559-19567%26journal%3DJ.%2BPhys.%2BChem.%2BC%26issue%3D36%26author%3DT.%2BS.%2BWang%26author%3DN.%2BQ.%2BZhao%26author%3DC.%2BS.%2BShi%26author%3DL.%2BY.%2BMa%26author%3DF.%2BHe%26author%3DC.%2BN.%2BHe%26author%3DJ.%2BJ.%2BLi%26author%3DE.%2BZ.%2BLiu%26title%3DInterface%2Band%2BDoping%2BEffects%2Bon%2BLi%2BIon%2BStorage%2BBehavior%2Bof%2BGraphene%252FLi2O%26doi%3D10.1021%252Facs.jpcc.7b04642&amp;doi=10.1021%2Facs.nanolett.0c02595&amp;doiOfLink=10.1021%2Facs.jpcc.7b04642&amp;linkType=gs&amp;linkLocation=Reference&amp;linkSource=FULL_TEXT" target="_blank">Google Scholar</a></span><div class="casRecord" id="
                        cas_cit17R"><div class="casContent"><span class="casTitleNuber">17</span><div class="casTitle"><span class="NLM_cas:atitle">Interface and Doping Effects on Li Ion Storage Behavior of Graphene/Li2O</span></div><div class="casAuthors">Wang, Tianshuai; Zhao, Naiqin; Shi, Chunsheng; Ma, Liying; He, Fang; He, Chunnian; Li, Jiajun; Liu, Enzuo</div><div class="citationInfo"><span class="NLM_cas:title">Journal of Physical Chemistry C</span>
        (<span class="NLM_cas:date">2017</span>),
    <span class="NLM_cas:volume">121</span>
        (<span class="NLM_cas:issue">36</span>),
    <span class="NLM_cas:pages">19559-19567</span>CODEN:
                <span class="NLM_cas:coden">JPCCCK</span>;
        ISSN:<span class="NLM_cas:issn">1932-7447</span>.
    
            (<span class="NLM_cas:orgname">American Chemical Society</span>)
        </div><div class="casAbstract">Graphene/metal oxide nanocomposites have been widely used as the anode materials for Li ion batteries, which exhibit much higher Li storage capacity beyond their theor. capacity.  In order to make clear the Li storage mechanism in graphene/metal oxide, we systematically investigated the interface and (B, N, O, S) doping effects on Li ion storage behavior in graphene/Li2O using first-principles total energy calcns.  It is revealed that the doping elements increase the van der Waals interface interaction of graphene/Li2O by changing the electronic structure of graphene through different mechanisms.  The Li storage at the graphene/Li2O interface exhibits the synergistic effect resulting from the enhanced interface interaction by the Li insertion at the interface.  The p-type and n-type doping induced by B and N dopants in graphene enhance and reduce the Li storage capability of graphene/Li2O, resp.  O and S doping result in the localization of the electronic states in graphene which benefits the Li adsorption at the interface.  The localization of electronic states combined with the appropriate dopant electronegativity can enhance the Li atoms adsorption and diffusion simultaneously.  Thereby, the highest interfacial lithium storage (0.330 mhA/m2) is obtained for the O-doped system, while the S-doped system possesses the good balance between interfacial Li storage (0.220 mhA/m2) and diffusion energy barrier (0.27 eV).  The results open a new insight for the design of graphene/metal oxide composites as energy storage materials.</div></div></div></div></div></div></li><li id="ref18"><div class="reference"><strong class="refLabel"><a href="javascript:void(0);" data-citation="" data-rid="ref18" class="refNumLink">18</a></strong><div class="NLM_citation references__item" id="cit18" data-doi="10.1002/aenm.201600467"><span><span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Gao, H.                         cas_cit20R"><div class="casContent"><span class="casTitleNuber">20</span><div class="casTitle"><span class="NLM_cas:atitle">Sodiation Kinetics of Metal Oxide Conversion Electrodes: A Comparative Study with Lithiation</span></div><div class="casAuthors">He, Kai; Lin, Feng; Zhu, Yizhou; Yu, Xiqian; Li, Jing; Lin, Ruoqian; Nordlund, Dennis; Weng, Tsu-Chien; Richards, Ryan M.; Yang, Xiao-Qing; Doeff, Marca M.; Stach, Eric A.; Mo, Yifei; Xin, Huolin L.; Su, Dong</div><div class="citationInfo"><span class="NLM_cas:title">Nano Letters</span>
        (<span class="NLM_cas:date">2015</span>),
    <span class="NLM_cas:volume">15</span>
        (<span class="NLM_cas:issue">9</span>),
    <span class="NLM_cas:pages">5755-5763</span>CODEN:
                <span class="NLM_cas:coden">NALEFD</span>;
        ISSN:<span class="NLM_cas:issn">1530-6984</span>.
    
            (<span class="NLM_cas:orgname">American Chemical Society</span>)
        </div><div class="casAbstract">The development of sodium ion batteries (NIBs) can provide an alternative to lithium ion batteries (LIBs) for sustainable, low-cost energy storage.  However, due to the larger size and higher m/e ratio of the sodium ion compared to lithium, sodiation reactions of candidate electrodes are expected to differ in significant ways from the corresponding lithium ones.  In this work, we investigated the sodiation mechanism of a typical transition metal-oxide, NiO, through a set of correlated techniques, including electrochem. and synchrotron studies, real-time electron microscopy observation, and ab initio mol. dynamics (MD) simulations.  We found that a cryst. Na2O reaction layer that was formed at the beginning of sodiation plays an important role in blocking the further transport of sodium ions.  In addn., sodiation in NiO exhibits a "shrinking-core" mode that results from a layer-by-layer reaction, as identified by ab initio MD simulations.  For lithiation, however, the formation of Li antisite defects significantly distorts the local NiO lattice that facilitates Li insertion, thus enhancing the overall reaction rate.  These observations delineate the mechanistic difference between sodiation and lithiation in metal-oxide conversion materials.  More importantly, our findings identify the importance of understanding the role of reaction layers on the functioning of electrodes and thus provide crit. insights into further optimizing NIB materials through surface engineering.</div></div></div></div></div></div></li><li id="ref21"><div class="reference"><strong class="refLabel"><a href="javascript:void(0);" data-citation="" data-rid="ref21" class="refNumLink">21</a></strong><div class="NLM_citation references__item" id="cit21" data-doi="10.1002/adma.201500196"><span><span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Zhang, K.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Hu, Z.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Liu, X.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Tao, Z.                         cas_cit21R"><div class="casContent"><span class="casTitleNuber">21</span><div class="casTitle"><span class="NLM_cas:atitle">FeSe2 Microspheres as a High-Performance Anode Material for Na-Ion Batteries</span></div><div class="casAuthors">Zhang, Kai; Hu, Zhe; Liu, Xue; Tao, Zhanliang; Chen, Jun</div><div class="citationInfo"><span class="NLM_cas:title">Advanced Materials (Weinheim, Germany)</span>
        (<span class="NLM_cas:date">2015</span>),
    <span class="NLM_cas:volume">27</span>
        (<span class="NLM_cas:issue">21</span>),
    <span class="NLM_cas:pages">3305-3309</span>CODEN:

        </div><div class="casAbstract">The use is demonstrated of FeSe2 as an anode material for sodium-ion batteries.  The FeSe2 microspheres assembled by nanooctahedra display a high tapping d. of 1.85 g cm-3 and a discharge capacity of 447 mA h g-1 at 0.1 A g-1.  The FeSe2 anode also exhibits long-term cyclic stability (372 mA h g-1 with capacity retention of 89.0% after 2000 cycles at 1 A g-1 ) and excellent high-rate performance 388 mA h g-1 at 10 C, 337 mA h g-1 at 20 C, 226 mA h g-1 at 50 C, where 1 C = 0.5 A g-1.  The conversion reaction of FeSe2 + 4Na+ + 4e ↔ Fe + 2Na2Se has been confirmed.  Furthermore, the FeSe2 anode is coupled with an excessive Na3V2(PO4)3 cathode (the capacity of the Na3V2(PO4)3 is 1.5-2 times more than that of FeSe2) to fabricate a full cell, which exhibits a discharge capacity of 366 mA h g-1 based on the mass of FeSe2 with an av. output voltage of about 1.7 V at 1 A g-1.</div></div></div></div></div></div></li><li id="ref22"><div class="reference"><strong class="refLabel"><a href="javascript:void(0);" data-citation="" data-rid="ref22" class="refNumLink">22</a></strong><div class="NLM_citation references__item" id="cit22" data-doi="10.1002/adfm.201402833"><span><span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Zhang, Y.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Zhu, P.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Huang, L.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Xie, J.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Zhang, S.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Cao, G.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Zhao, X.                 10.1002/adfm.201402833</span> </span><div class=" links-group getFTR"><div class="getFTR__content getFTR__placeholder"></div><span class="references__suffix"><a class="google-scholar" href="/action/getFTRLinkout?url=http%3A%2F%2Fscholar.google.com%2Fscholar_lookup%3Fhl%3Den%26volume%3D25%26publication_year%3D2015%26pages%3D481-489%26journal%3DAdv.%2BFunct.%2BMater.%26issue%3D3%26author%3DY.%2BZhang%26author%3DP.%2BZhu%26author%3DL.%2BHuang%26author%3DJ.%2BXie%26author%3DS.%2BZhang%26author%3DG.%2BCao%26author%3DX.%2BB.%2BZhao%26title%3DFew-Layered%2BSnS2%2Bon%2BFew-Layered%2BReduced%2BGraphene%2BOxide%2Bas%2BNa-Ion%2BBattery%2BAnode%2Bwith%2BUltralong%2BCycle%2BLife%2Band%2BSuperior%2BRate%2BCapability%26doi%3D10.1002%252Fadfm.201402833&amp;doi=10.1021%2Facs.nanolett.0c02595&amp;doiOfLink=10.1002%2Fadfm.201402833&amp;linkType=gs&amp;linkLocation=Reference&amp;linkSource=FULL_TEXT" target="_blank">Google Scholar</a></span><div class="casRecord" id="
                        cas_cit22R"><div class="casContent"><span class="casTitleNuber">22</span><div class="casTitle"><span class="NLM_cas:atitle">Few-Layered SnS2 on Few-Layered Reduced Graphene Oxide as Na-Ion Battery Anode with Ultralong Cycle Life and Superior Rate Capability</span></div><div class="casAuthors">Zhang, Yandong; Zhu, Peiyi; Huang, Liliang; Xie, Jian; Zhang, Shichao; Cao, Gaoshao; Zhao, Xinbing</div><div class="citationInfo"><span class="NLM_cas:title">Advanced Functional Materials</span>
        (<span class="NLM_cas:date">2015</span>),
    <span class="NLM_cas:volume">25</span>
        (<span class="NLM_cas:issue">3</span>),
    <span class="NLM_cas:pages">481-489</span>CODEN:
                <span class="NLM_cas:coden">AFMDC6</span>;
        ISSN:<span class="NLM_cas:issn">1616-301X</span>.
    
            (<span class="NLM_cas:orgname">Wiley-VCH Verlag GmbH &amp; Co. KGaA</span>)
        </div><div class="casAbstract">Na-ion batteries have been considered as promising alternatives to Li-ion batteries due to the natural abundance of sodium resources.  Searching for high-performance anode materials currently becomes a hot topic and also a great challenge for developing Na-ion batteries.  In this work, a novel hybrid anode is synthesized consisting of ultrafine, few-layered SnS2 anchored on few-layered reduced graphene oxide (rGO) by a facile solvothermal route.  The SnS2/rGO hybrid exhibits a high capacity, ultralong cycle life, and superior rate capability.  The hybrid can deliver a high charge capacity of 649 mA-h/g at 100 mA/g.  At 800 mA/g (1.8C), it can yield an initial charge capacity of 469 mA-h/g, which can be maintained at 89% and 61%, resp., after 400 and 1000 cycles.  The hybrid can also sustain a c.d. up to 12.8 A/g (≈28C) where the charge process can be completed in only 1.3 min while still delivering a charge capacity of 337 mA-h/g.  The fast and stable Na-storage ability of SnS2/rGO makes it a promising anode for Na-ion batteries.</div></div></div></div></div></div></li><li id="ref23"><div class="reference"><strong class="refLabel"><a href="javascript:void(0);" data-citation="" data-rid="ref23" class="refNumLink">23</a></strong><div class="NLM_citation references__item" id="cit23" data-doi="10.1002/adma.201704670"><span><span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Qin, J.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Wang, T.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Liu, D.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Liu, E.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Zhao, N.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Shi, C.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">He, F.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Ma, L.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">He, C.</span></span> <span> </span><span class="NLM_article-title">A Top-Down Strategy toward SnSb In-Plane Nanoconfined 3D N-Doped Porous Graphene Composite Microspheres for High Performance Na-Ion Battery Anode</span>. <i>Adv.                         cas_cit24R"><div class="casContent"><span class="casTitleNuber">24</span><div class="casTitle"><span class="NLM_cas:atitle">Electrostatic Self-Assembly Enabling Integrated Bulk and Interfacial Sodium Storage in 3D Titania-Graphene Hybrid</span></div><div class="casAuthors">Xu, Gui-Liang; Xiao, Lisong; Sheng, Tian; Liu, Jianzhao; Hu, Yi-Xin; Ma, Tianyuan; Amine, Rachid; Xie, Yingying; Zhang, Xiaoyi; Liu, Yuzi; Ren, Yang; Sun, Cheng-Jun; Heald, Steve M.; Kovacevic, Jasmina; Sehlleier, Yee Hwa; Schulz, Christof; Mattis, Wenjuan Liu; Sun, Shi-Gang; Wiggers, Hartmut; Chen, Zonghai; Amine, Khalil</div><div class="citationInfo"><span class="NLM_cas:title">Nano Letters</span>
        (<span class="NLM_cas:date">2018</span>),
    <span class="NLM_cas:volume">18</span>
        (<span class="NLM_cas:issue">1</span>),
    <span class="NLM_cas:pages">336-346</span>CODEN:

            (<span class="NLM_cas:orgname">American Chemical Society</span>)
        </div><div class="casAbstract">Room-temp. sodium-ion batteries have attracted increased attention for energy storage due to the natural abundance of sodium.  However, it remains a huge challenge to develop versatile electrode materials with favorable properties, which requires smart structure design and good mechanistic understanding.  Herein, we reported a general and scalable approach to synthesize three-dimensional (3D) titania-graphene hybrid via electrostatic-interaction-induced self-assembly.  Synchrotron X-ray probe, transmission electron microscopy, and computational modeling revealed that the strong interaction between titania and graphene through comparably strong van der Waals forces not only facilitates bulk Na+ intercalation but also enhances the interfacial sodium storage.  As a result, the titania-graphene hybrid exhibits exceptional long-term cycle stability up to 5000 cycles, and ultrahigh rate capability up to 20 C for sodium storage.  Furthermore, d. function theory calcn. indicated that the interfacial Li+, K+, Mg2+, and Al3+ storage can be enhanced as well.  The proposed general strategy opens up new avenues to create versatile materials for advanced battery systems.</div></div></div></div></div></div></li><li id="ref25"><div class="reference"><strong class="refLabel"><a href="javascript:void(0);" data-citation="" data-rid="ref25" class="refNumLink">25</a></strong><div class="NLM_citation references__item" id="cit25" data-doi="10.1016/j.jechem.2018.09.001"><span><span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Huang, J.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Wei, Z.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Liao, J.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Ni, W.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Wang, C.                         cas_cit26R"><div class="casContent"><span class="casTitleNuber">26</span><div class="casTitle"><span class="NLM_cas:atitle">New Mechanistic Insights on Na-Ion Storage in Nongraphitizable Carbon</span></div><div class="casAuthors">Bommier, Clement; Surta, Todd Wesley; Dolgos, Michelle; Ji, Xiulei</div><div class="citationInfo"><span class="NLM_cas:title">Nano Letters</span>
        (<span class="NLM_cas:date">2015</span>),
    <span class="NLM_cas:volume">15</span>
        (<span class="NLM_cas:issue">9</span>),
    <span class="NLM_cas:pages">5888-5892</span>CODEN:
                <span class="NLM_cas:coden">NALEFD</span>;
        ISSN:<span class="NLM_cas:issn">1530-6984</span>.
    
            (<span class="NLM_cas:orgname">American Chemical Society</span>)
        </div><div class="casAbstract">Nongraphitizable carbon, also known as hard carbon, is considered one of the most promising anodes for the emerging Na-ion batteries.  The current mechanistic understanding of Na-ion storage in hard carbon is based on the "card-house" model first raised in the early 2000s.  This model describes that Na-ion insertion occurs first through intercalation between graphene sheets in turbostratic nanodomains, followed by Na filling of the pores in the carbon structure.  We tried to test this model by tuning the sizes of turbostratic nanodomains but revealed a correlation between the structural defects and Na-ion storage.  Based on our exptl. data, we propose an alternative perspective for sodiation of hard carbon that consists of Na-ion storage at defect sites, by intercalation and last via pore-filling.</div></div></div></div></div></div></li><li id="ref27"><div class="reference"><strong class="refLabel"><a href="javascript:void(0);" data-citation="" data-rid="ref27" class="refNumLink">27</a></strong><div class="NLM_citation references__item" id="cit27" data-doi="10.1021/acsnano.9b00375"><span><span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Ou, X.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Cao, L.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Liang, X.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Zheng, F.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Zheng, H.                 10.1021/acsnano.9b00375</span> </span><div class=" links-group getFTR"><div class="getFTR__content getFTR__placeholder"></div><span class="references__suffix"><a class="google-scholar" href="/action/getFTRLinkout?url=http%3A%2F%2Fscholar.google.com%2Fscholar_lookup%3Fhl%3Den%26volume%3D13%26publication_year%3D2019%26pages%3D3666-3676%26journal%3DACS%2BNano%26author%3DX.%2BOu%26author%3DL.%2BCao%26author%3DX.%2BLiang%26author%3DF.%2BZheng%26author%3DH.%2BS.%2BZheng%26author%3DX.%2BYang%26author%3DJ.%2BWang%26author%3DC.%2BYang%26author%3DM.%2BL.%2BLiu%26title%3DFabrication%2Bof%2BSnS2%252FMn2SnS4%252FCarbon%2BHeterostructures%2Bfor%2BSodium-Ion%2BBatteries%2Bwith%2BHigh%2BInitial%2BCoulombic%2BEfficiency%2Band%2BCycling%2BStability%26doi%3D10.1021%252Facsnano.9b00375&amp;doi=10.1021%2Facs.nanolett.0c02595&amp;doiOfLink=10.1021%2Facsnano.9b00375&amp;linkType=gs&amp;linkLocation=Reference&amp;linkSource=FULL_TEXT" target="_blank">Google Scholar</a></span><div class="casRecord" id="
                        cas_cit27R"><div class="casContent"><span class="casTitleNuber">27</span><div class="casTitle"><span class="NLM_cas:atitle">Fabrication of SnS2/Mn2SnS4/Carbon Heterostructures for Sodium-Ion Batteries with High Initial Coulombic Efficiency and Cycling Stability</span></div><div class="casAuthors">Ou, Xing; Cao, Liang; Liang, Xinghui; Zheng, Fenghua; Zheng, Hong-Sheng; Yang, Xianfeng; Wang, Jeng-Han; Yang, Chenghao; Liu, Meilin</div><div class="citationInfo"><span class="NLM_cas:title">ACS Nano</span>
        (<span class="NLM_cas:date">2019</span>),
    <span class="NLM_cas:volume">13</span>
        (<span class="NLM_cas:issue">3</span>),
    <span class="NLM_cas:pages">3666-3676</span>CODEN:
                <span class="NLM_cas:coden">ANCAC3</span>;
        ISSN:<span class="NLM_cas:issn">1936-0851</span>.
    
            (<span class="NLM_cas:orgname">American Chemical Society</span>)
        </div><div class="casAbstract">SnS2 has been extensive studied as an anode material for sodium storage owing to its high theor. specific capacity, whereas the unsatisfied initial Coulombic efficiency (ICE) caused by the partial irreversible conversion reaction during the charge/discharge process is one of the crit. issues that hamper its practical applications.  Hence, heterostructured SnS2/Mn2SnS4/carbon nanoboxes (SMS/C NBs) have been developed by a facial wet-chem. method and utilized as the anode material of sodium ion batteries.  SMS/C NBs can deliver an initial capacity of 841.2 mAh g-1 with high ICE of 90.8%, excellent rate capability (752.3, 604.7, 570.1, 546.9, 519.7, and 488.7 mAh g-1 at the current rate of 0.1, 0.5, 1.0, 2.0, 5.0, and 10.0 A g-1, resp.), and long cycling stability (522.5 mAh g-1 at 5.0 A g-1 after 500 cycles).  The existence of SnS2/Mn2SnS4 heterojunctions can effectively stabilize the reaction products Sn and Na2S, greatly prevent the coarsening of nanosized Sn0, and enhance reversible conversion--alloying reaction, which play a key role in improving the ICE and extending the cycling performance.  Moreover, the heterostructured SMS coupled with the interacting carbon network provides efficient channels for electrons and Na+ diffusion, resulting in an excellent rate performance.</div></div></div></div></div></div></li><li id="ref28"><div class="reference"><strong class="refLabel"><a href="javascript:void(0);" data-citation="" data-rid="ref28" class="refNumLink">28</a></strong><div class="NLM_citation references__item" id="cit28" data-doi="10.1002/adfm.201908665"><span><span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Deng, Q.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Chen, F.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Liu, S.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Bayaguud, A.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Feng, Y.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Zhang, Z.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Fu, Y.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Yu, Y.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Zhu, C.                         cas_cit33R"><div class="casContent"><span class="casTitleNuber">33</span><div class="casTitle"><span class="NLM_cas:atitle">Unique Double-Interstitialcy Mechanism and Interfacial Storage Mechanism in the Graphene/Metal Oxide as the Anode for Sodium-Ion Batteries</span></div><div class="casAuthors">Wang, Tianshuai; Qu, Jiale; Legut, Dominik; Qin, Jian; Li, Xifei; Zhang, Qianfan</div><div class="citationInfo"><span class="NLM_cas:title">Nano Letters</span>
        (<span class="NLM_cas:date">2019</span>),
    <span class="NLM_cas:volume">19</span>

            (<span class="NLM_cas:orgname">American Chemical Society</span>)
        </div><div class="casAbstract">Graphene/metal oxides (G/MO) composite materials have attracted much attention as the anode of Na ion batteries (SIBs), because of the high theor. capacity.  However, most metal oxides operate based on the conversion mechanism and the alloying mechanism has changed to Na2O after the 1st cycle.  The influence of G/Na2O (G/N) on the subsequent sodiation process has never been clearly elucidated.  The authors report a systematic study on the G/N interface from both aspects of theor. simulation and expt. characterization.  By applied 1st-principles simulations, the sluggish kinetics in the G/MO materials is mainly caused by the high diffusion barrier (0.51 eV) inside the Na2O bulk, while the G/N interface shows a much faster transport kinetics (0.25 eV) via unique double-interstitialcy mechanism.  G/N interface possesses an interfacial storage of Na atom through the charge sepn. mechanism.  The exptl. evidence confirms that high interfacial ratio structure of G/N greatly improves the rate performance and endows G/MO materials the interfacial storage.  Also, the exptl. study finds that the high interfacial ratio structure of G/N also benefits from the reversible reaction between SnO2 and Sn during cycling.  Lastly, the effects of (N, O, S) doping in graphene systems at the G/N interface were also explored.  This work provides a fundamental comprehension on the G/MO interface structure during the sodiation process, which is helpful to design energy storage materials with high rate performance and large capacity.</div></div></div></div></div></div></li><li id="ref34"><div class="reference"><strong class="refLabel"><a href="javascript:void(0);" data-citation="" data-rid="ref34" class="refNumLink">34</a></strong><div class="NLM_citation references__item" id="cit34" data-doi="10.1002/adma.201806304"><span><span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Jin, T.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Han, Q.                         cas_cit34R"><div class="casContent"><span class="casTitleNuber">34</span><div class="casTitle"><span class="NLM_cas:atitle">Binder-Free Electrodes for Advanced Sodium-Ion Batteries</span></div><div class="casAuthors">Jin, Ting; Han, Qingqing; Jiao, Lifang</div><div class="citationInfo"><span class="NLM_cas:title">Advanced Materials (Weinheim, Germany)</span>

            (<span class="NLM_cas:orgname">Wiley-VCH Verlag GmbH &amp; Co. KGaA</span>)
        </div><div class="casAbstract">Sodium-ion batteries (SIBs) have recently emerged as one of the favored contenders for use in medium and large-scale stationary energy storage owing to the abundance of the resources required to fabricate them, their low cost, and the fact that have properties similar to equiv. Li batteries.  However, their development also faces challenges such as poor cycling stability and unsatisfying rate performance.  In traditional electrodes, binders are commonly used to integrate individual active materials with conductive additives.  Unfortunately, binders are generally electrochem. inactive and insulating, which reduces the overall energy d. and leads to poor cycling stability.  Therefore, binder-free electrodes provide great opportunity for high-performance SIBs in terms of both improved electronic cond. and electrochem. reaction reversibility.  This Progress Report provides an overview of the recent progress in binder-free electrodes for SIBs.                         cas_cit35R"><div class="casContent"><span class="casTitleNuber">35</span><div class="casTitle"><span class="NLM_cas:atitle">Assembly of SnSe Nanoparticles Confined in Graphene for Enhanced Sodium-Ion Storage Performance</span></div><div class="casAuthors">Yang, Xu; Zhang, Rongyu; Chen, Nan; Meng, Xing; Yang, Peilei; Wang, Chunzhong; Zhang, Yaoqing; Wei, Yingjin; Chen, Gang; Du, Fei</div><div class="citationInfo"><span class="NLM_cas:title">Chemistry - A European Journal</span>

    <span class="NLM_cas:volume">22</span>

        </div><div class="casAbstract">Sodium-ion batteries (SIBs) have attracted much interest as a low-cost and environmentally benign energy storage system, but more attention is justifiably required to address the major tech. issues relating to the anode materials to deliver high reversible capacity, superior rate capability, and stable cyclability.  A SnSe/reduced graphene oxide (RGO) nanocomposite has been prepd. by a facile ball-milling method, and its structural, morphol., and electrochem. properties have been characterized and compared with those of the bare SnSe material.  Although the redox behavior of SnSe remains nearly unchanged upon the incorporation of RGO, its electrochem. performance is significantly enhanced, as reflected by a high specific capacity of 590 mA h g-1 at 0.050 A g-1, a rate capability of 260 mA h g-1 at 10 A g-1, and long-term stability over 120 cycles.  This improvement may be attributed to the high electronic cond. of RGO, which also serves as a matrix to buffer changes in vol. and maintain the mech. integrity of the electrode during (de)sodiation processes.  In view of its excellent Na+ storage performance, this SnSe/RGO nanocomposite has potential as an anode material for SIBs.</div></div></div></div></div></div></li><li id="ref36"><div class="reference"><strong class="refLabel"><a href="javascript:void(0);" data-citation="" data-rid="ref36" class="refNumLink">36</a></strong><div class="NLM_citation references__item" id="cit36" data-doi="10.1016/j.carbon.2018.04.003"><span><span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Xue, P.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Wang, N.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Wang, Y.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Zhang, Y.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Liu, Y.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Tang, B.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Bai, Z.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Dou, S.                         cas_cit36R"><div class="casContent"><span class="casTitleNuber">36</span><div class="casTitle"><span class="NLM_cas:atitle">Nanoconfined SnS in 3D interconnected macroporous carbon as durable anodes for lithium/sodium ion batteries</span></div><div class="casAuthors">Xue, Pan; Wang, Nana; Wang, Yunxiao; Zhang, Yaohui; Liu, Yuanlin; Tang, Bin; Bai, Zhongchao; Dou, Shixue</div><div class="citationInfo"><span class="NLM_cas:title">Carbon</span>

        </div><div class="casAbstract">Nanoconfined SnS in 3D interconnected macroporous carbon (3D SnS/C) has been produced using silica opals as template following a carbonization and sulfuration route.  The electrochem. properties of the 3D SnS/C were examd. comprehensively as anode materials for lithium/sodium ion batteries (LIBs/SIBs).  It delivers a high specific capacity of 869mA h g-1 at 1 A g-1 after 1000 cycles in LIBs and 400 mA h g-1 at 100 mA g-1 after 100 cycles in SIBs.  The rate performance is also excellent (550 mA h g-1 at 3 A g-1 in LIBs and 220.9 mA h g-1 at 5 A g-1 in SIBs).  The outstanding electrochem. performance of the 3D SnS/C is ascribed to its 3D porous carbon interconnected structure and nanoconfined SnS nanoparticles distributing broadly in carbon matrix, which not only improve the cond., but also keep the structure integrity, and as a result of enhancing the cycling stability of the material.  In addn., this facile and novelty strategy can be potentially utilized for prepg. other 3D metal sulfides interconnected macroporous carbon composite for energy storage.</div></div></div></div></div></div></li><li id="ref37"><div class="reference"><strong class="refLabel"><a href="javascript:void(0);" data-citation="" data-rid="ref37" class="refNumLink">37</a></strong><div class="NLM_citation references__item" id="cit37" data-doi="10.1002/advs.201500200"><span><span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Zhu, C.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Kopold, P.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Li, W.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">van Aken, P.                         cas_cit37R"><div class="casContent"><span class="casTitleNuber">37</span><div class="casTitle"><span class="NLM_cas:atitle">A General Strategy to Fabricate Carbon-Coated 3D Porous Interconnected Metal Sulfides: Case Study of SnS/C Nanocomposite for High-Performance Lithium and Sodium Ion Batteries</span></div><div class="casAuthors">Zhu Changbao; Kopold Peter; van Aken Peter A; Maier Joachim; Li Weihan; Yu Yan</div><div class="citationInfo"><span class="NLM_cas:title">Advanced science (Weinheim, Baden-Wurttemberg, Germany)</span>

    </div><div class="casAbstract">Transition metal sulfides have a great potential for energy storage due to the pronouncedly higher capacity (owing to conversion to metal or even alloy) than traditional insertion electrode materials.  However, the poor cycling stability still limits the development and application in lithium and sodium ion batteries.  Here, taking SnS as a model material, a novel general strategy is proposed to fabricate a 3D porous interconnected metal sulfide/carbon nanocomposite by the electrostatic spray deposition technique without adding any expensive carbonaceous materials such as graphene or carbon nanotube.  In this way, small nanorods of SnS are generated with sizes of ≈10-20 nm embedded in amorphous carbon and self-assembled into a 3D porous interconnected nanocomposite.  The SnS:C is directly deposited on the Ti foil as a current collector and neither conductive additives nor binder are needed for battery assembly.  Such electrodes exhibit a high reversible capacity, high rate capability, and long cycling stability for both lithium and sodium storage.</div></div></div></div></div></div></li><li id="ref38"><div class="reference"><strong class="refLabel"><a href="javascript:void(0);" data-citation="" data-rid="ref38" class="refNumLink">38</a></strong><div class="NLM_citation references__item" id="cit38" data-doi="10.1016/j.ensm.2018.08.005"><span><span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Xia, J.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Liu, L.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Jamil, S.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Xie, J.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Yan, H.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Yuan, Y.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Zhang, Y.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Nie, S.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Pan, J.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Wang, X.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Cao, G.                         cas_cit40R"><div class="casContent"><span class="casTitleNuber">40</span><div class="casTitle"><span class="NLM_cas:atitle">Ordered mesoporous α-MoO3 with iso-oriented nanocrystalline walls for thin-film pseudocapacitors</span></div><div class="casAuthors">Brezesinski, Torsten; Wang, John; Tolbert, Sarah H.; Dunn, Bruce</div><div class="citationInfo"><span class="NLM_cas:title">Nature Materials</span>
        (<span class="NLM_cas:date">2010</span>),
    <span class="NLM_cas:volume">9</span>
        (<span class="NLM_cas:issue">2</span>),

            (<span class="NLM_cas:orgname">Nature Publishing Group</span>)
        </div><div class="casAbstract">Capacitive energy storage is distinguished from other types of electrochem. energy storage by short charging times and the ability to deliver significantly more power than batteries.  A limitation to this technol. is its low energy d. and there is interest in exploring pseudo-capacitive materials where faradaic mechanisms offer increased energy storage.  The capacitive charge-storage properties of mesoporous films of iso-oriented α-MoO3 are superior to those of either mesoporous amorphous material or nonporous cryst. MoO3.  Whereas both cryst. and amorphous mesoporous materials show redox pseudo-capacitance, the iso-oriented layered cryst. domains enable Li ions to be inserted into the van der Waals gaps of the α-MoO3.  Probably this extra contribution arises from an intercalation pseudo-capacitance, which occurs on the same timescale as redox pseudo-capacitance.  The result is increased charge-storage capacity without compromising charge/discharge kinetics in mesoporous cryst. MoO3.</div></div></div></div></div></div></li><li id="ref41"><div class="reference"><strong class="refLabel"><a href="javascript:void(0);" data-citation="" data-rid="ref41" class="refNumLink">41</a></strong><div class="NLM_citation references__item" id="cit41" data-doi="10.1038/ncomms12122"><span><span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Chao, D.                 10.1038/ncomms12122</span> </span><div class=" links-group getFTR"><div class="getFTR__content getFTR__placeholder"></div><span class="references__suffix"><a class="google-scholar" href="/action/getFTRLinkout?url=http%3A%2F%2Fscholar.google.com%2Fscholar_lookup%3Fhl%3Den%26volume%3D7%26publication_year%3D2016%26pages%3D12122%26journal%3DNat.%2BCommun.%26author%3DD.%2BL.%2BChao%26author%3DC.%2BR.%2BZhu%26author%3DP.%2BH.%2BYang%26author%3DX.%2BXia%26author%3DJ.%2BLiu%26author%3DJ.%2BWang%26author%3DX.%2BFan%26author%3DS.%2BV.%2BSavilov%26author%3DJ.%2BY.%2BLin%26author%3DH.%2BFan%26author%3DZ.%2BX.%2BShen%26title%3DArray%2Bof%2BNanosheets%2BRender%2BUltrafast%2Band%2BHigh-Capacity%2BNa-Ion%2BStorage%2Bby%2BTunable%2BPseudocapacitance%26doi%3D10.1038%252Fncomms12122&amp;doi=10.1021%2Facs.nanolett.0c02595&amp;doiOfLink=10.1038%2Fncomms12122&amp;linkType=gs&amp;linkLocation=Reference&amp;linkSource=FULL_TEXT" target="_blank">Google Scholar</a></span><div class="casRecord" id="
                        cas_cit41R"><div class="casContent"><span class="casTitleNuber">41</span><div class="casTitle"><span class="NLM_cas:atitle">Array of nanosheets render ultrafast and high-capacity Na-ion storage by tunable pseudocapacitance</span></div><div class="casAuthors">Chao, Dongliang; Zhu, Changrong; Yang, Peihua; Xia, Xinhui; Liu, Jilei; Wang, Jin; Fan, Xiaofeng; Savilov, Serguei V.; Lin, Jianyi; Fan, Hong Jin; Shen, Ze Xiang</div><div class="citationInfo"><span class="NLM_cas:title">Nature Communications</span>
        (<span class="NLM_cas:date">2016</span>),
    <span class="NLM_cas:volume">7</span>
        (<span class="NLM_cas:issue"></span>),
    <span class="NLM_cas:pages">12122</span>CODEN:
                <span class="NLM_cas:coden">NCAOBW</span>;
        ISSN:<span class="NLM_cas:issn">2041-1723</span>.
    
            (<span class="NLM_cas:orgname">Nature Publishing Group</span>)
        </div><div class="casAbstract">Sodium-ion batteries are a potentially low-cost and safe alternative to the prevailing lithium-ion battery technol.  However, it is a great challenge to achieve fast charging and high power d. for most sodium-ion electrodes because of the sluggish sodiation kinetics.  Here we demonstrate a high-capacity and high-rate sodium-ion anode based on ultrathin layered tin(II) sulfide nanostructures, in which a maximized extrinsic pseudocapacitance contribution is identified and verified by kinetics anal.  The graphene foam supported tin(II) sulfide nanoarray anode delivers a high reversible capacity of ∼1,100 mAh g-1 at 30 mA g-1 and ∼420 mAh g-1 at 30 A g-1, which even outperforms its lithium-ion storage performance.  The surface-dominated redox reaction rendered by our tailored ultrathin tin(II) sulfide nanostructures may also work in other layered materials for high-performance sodium-ion storage.</div></div></div></div></div></div></li></ol></div><script type="text/javascript" nonce="93187e55dd07075b-SJC">window.refViewer={'ref1':['cit1'],'ref2':['cit2'],'ref3':['cit3'],'ref4':['cit4'],'ref5':['cit5'],'ref6':['cit6'],'ref7':['cit7'],'ref8':['cit8'],'ref9':['cit9'],'ref10':['cit10'],'ref11':['cit11'],'ref12':['cit12'],'ref13':['cit13'],'ref14':['cit14'],'ref15':['cit15'],'ref16':['cit16'],'ref17':['cit17'],'ref18':['cit18'],'ref19':['cit19'],'ref20':['cit20'],'ref21':['cit21'],'ref22':['cit22'],'ref23':['cit23'],'ref24':['cit24'],'ref25':['cit25'],'ref26':['cit26'],'ref27':['cit27'],'ref28':['cit28'],'ref29':['cit29'],'ref30':['cit30'],'ref31':['cit31'],'ref32':['cit32'],'ref33':['cit33'],'ref34':['cit34'],'ref35':['cit35'],'ref36':['cit36'],'ref37':['cit37'],'ref38':['cit38'],'ref39':['cit39'],'ref40':['cit40'],'ref41':['cit41']}</script><div class="articleCitedByDropzone" data-pb-dropzone="articleCitedByDropzone"><div class="cited-by"><div class="article_content-header cited-content_header"><div class="article_content-header-row"><div tabindex="0" class="article_content-title"><h2 id="citeThis">Cited By</h2><div class="article__copy"> <button class="article__copy__btn"><i aria-hidden="true" class="article__copy__icon icon-copy-solid"></i></button><span class="article__copy__text">Click to copy section link</span><span class="article__copy__success">Section link copied!</span></div></div></div></div><div class="cited-by__content"><div class="sciteMetricsDropzone">

M.</span></span> <span> </span><span class="NLM_article-title">Nano-sized transition-metal oxides as negative-electrode materials for lithium-ion batteries</span>. <i>Nature</i>  <span class="NLM_year" style="font-weight: bold;">2000</span>,  <span class="NLM_volume"><i>407</i></span>,  <span class="NLM_fpage">496</span>– <span class="NLM_lpage">499</span>, <span class="refDoi">&nbsp;DOI:
                10.1038/35035045</span> </span><div class=" links-group getFTR"><div class="getFTR__content getFTR__placeholder"></div><div class="casRecord" id="
                        rightTab-cas_cit1R"><div class="casContent"><span class="casTitleNuber">1</span><div class="casTitle"><span class="NLM_cas:atitle">Nano-sized transition-metal oxides as negative-electrode materials for lithium-ion batteries</span></div><div class="casAuthors">Poizot P; Laruelle S; Grugeon S; Dupont L; Tarascon J M</div><div class="citationInfo"><span class="NLM_cas:title">Nature</span>
        (<span class="NLM_cas:date">2000</span>),
    <span class="NLM_cas:volume">407</span>
        (<span class="NLM_cas:issue">6803</span>),
    <span class="NLM_cas:pages">496-9</span>
        ISSN:<span class="NLM_cas:issn">0028-0836</span>.
    </div><div class="casAbstract">Rechargeable solid-state batteries have long been considered an attractive power source for a wide variety of applications, and in particular, lithium-ion batteries are emerging as the technology of choice for portable electronics.  One of the main challenges in the design of these batteries is to ensure that the electrodes maintain their integrity over many discharge-recharge cycles.  Although promising electrode systems have recently been proposed, their lifespans are limited by Li-alloying agglomeration or the growth of passivation layers, which prevent the fully reversible insertion of Li ions into the negative electrodes.  Here we report that electrodes made of nanoparticles of transition-metal oxides (MO, where M is Co, Ni, Cu or Fe) demonstrate electrochemical capacities of 700 mA h g(-1), with 100% capacity retention for up to 100 cycles and high recharging rates.  The mechanism of Li reactivity differs from the classical Li insertion/deinsertion or Li-alloying processes, and involves the formation and decomposition of Li2O, accompanying the reduction and oxidation of metal nanoparticles (in the range 1-5 nanometres) respectively.  We expect that the use of transition-metal nanoparticles to enhance surface electrochemical reactivity will lead to further improvements in the performance of lithium-ion batteries.</div></div></div></div></div></div></li><li id="rightTab-ref2"><div class="reference"><strong class="refLabel"><a href="#ref2" class="rightTabRefNumLink">2</a></strong><div class="NLM_citation references__item" id="rightTab-cit2" data-doi="10.1021/nl800957b"><span><span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Yoo, E. J.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Kim, J.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Hosono, E.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Zhou, H. S.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Kudo, T.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Honma, I.</span></span> <span> </span><span class="NLM_article-title">Large reversible Li storage of graphene nanosheet families for use in rechargeable lithium ion batteries</span>. <i>Nano Lett.</i>  <span class="NLM_year" style="font-weight: bold;">2008</span>,  <span class="NLM_volume"><i>8</i></span> (<span class="NLM_issue">8</span>),  <span class="NLM_fpage">2277</span>– <span class="NLM_lpage">2282</span>, <span class="refDoi">&nbsp;DOI:
                10.1021/nl800957b</span> </span><div class=" links-group getFTR"><div class="getFTR__content getFTR__placeholder"></div><div class="casRecord" id="
                        rightTab-cas_cit2R"><div class="casContent"><span class="casTitleNuber">2</span><div class="casTitle"><span class="NLM_cas:atitle">Large Reversible Li Storage of Graphene Nanosheet Families for Use in Rechargeable Lithium Ion Batteries</span></div><div class="casAuthors">Yoo, EunJoo; Kim, Jedeok; Hosono, Eiji; Zhou, Hao-shen; Kudo, Tetsuichi; Honma, Itaru</div><div class="citationInfo"><span class="NLM_cas:title">Nano Letters</span>
        (<span class="NLM_cas:date">2008</span>),
    <span class="NLM_cas:volume">8</span>
        (<span class="NLM_cas:issue">8</span>),
    <span class="NLM_cas:pages">2277-2282</span>CODEN:
                <span class="NLM_cas:coden">NALEFD</span>;
        ISSN:<span class="NLM_cas:issn">1530-6984</span>.
    
            (<span class="NLM_cas:orgname">American Chemical Society</span>)
        </div><div class="casAbstract">The lithium storage properties of graphene nanosheet materials as high capacity anode materials for lithium secondary batteries were investigated.  Graphite is a practical anode material used for lithium secondary batteries, because of its capability for reversible lithium ion intercalation in the layered crystals, and the structural similarities of graphene nanosheet to graphite may provide another type of intercalation anode compd.  While the accommodation of lithium in these layered compds. is influenced by the layer spacing between the graphene nanosheets, control of the intergraphene sheet distance through interacting mols. such as carbon nanotubes or fullerenes (C60) might be crucial for enhancement of the storage capacity.  The sp. capacity of graphene nanosheet was found to be 540 mA-h/g, which is much larger than that of graphite, and this was increased up to 730 mA-h/g and 784 mA-h/g, resp., by the incorporation of macromols. of carbon nanotubes and C60 to the graphene nanosheet.</div></div></div></div></div></div></li><li id="rightTab-ref3"><div class="reference"><strong class="refLabel"><a href="#ref3" class="rightTabRefNumLink">3</a></strong><div class="NLM_citation references__item" id="rightTab-cit3" data-doi="10.1002/anie.200500946"><span><span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Cao, A. M.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Hu, J. S.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Liang, H. P.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Wan, L. J.</span></span> <span> </span><span class="NLM_article-title">Self-Assembled Vanadium Pentoxide (V<sub>2</sub>O<sub>5</sub>) Hollow Microspheres from Nanorods and Their Application in Lithium-Ion Batteries</span>. <i>Angew. Chem., Int. Ed.</i>  <span class="NLM_year" style="font-weight: bold;">2005</span>,  <span class="NLM_volume"><i>44</i></span> (<span class="NLM_issue">28</span>),  <span class="NLM_fpage">4391</span>– <span class="NLM_lpage">4395</span>, <span class="refDoi">&nbsp;DOI:
                10.1002/anie.200500946</span> </span><div class=" links-group getFTR"><div class="getFTR__content getFTR__placeholder"></div><div class="casRecord" id="
                        rightTab-cas_cit3R"><div class="casContent"><span class="casTitleNuber">3</span><div class="casTitle"><span class="NLM_cas:atitle">Self-assembled vanadium pentoxide (V2O5) hollow microspheres from nanorods and their application in lithium-ion batteries</span></div><div class="casAuthors">Cao, An-Min; Hu, Jin-Song; Liang, Han-Pu; Wan, Li-Jun</div><div class="citationInfo"><span class="NLM_cas:title">Angewandte Chemie, International Edition</span>
        (<span class="NLM_cas:date">2005</span>),
    <span class="NLM_cas:volume">44</span>
        (<span class="NLM_cas:issue">28</span>),
    <span class="NLM_cas:pages">4391-4395</span>CODEN:
                <span class="NLM_cas:coden">ACIEF5</span>;
        ISSN:<span class="NLM_cas:issn">1433-7851</span>.
    
            (<span class="NLM_cas:orgname">Wiley-VCH Verlag GmbH &amp; Co. KGaA</span>)
        </div><div class="casAbstract">Hedgehog or nest microspheres of V2O5 are formed when nanorods of V2O5 are synthesized in a mediated polyol process.  When the concn. of the starting material is low, a nest shape is preferred, whereas a high concn. of vanadium(III) acetylacetonate affords hedgehog-shaped microspheres.         </div><div class="casAbstract">A review.  In the past few decades, great effort has been made toward the prepn. and development of advanced transition metal dichalcogenide (TMD) materials for anodes of alkali metal ion batteries (AMIBs).  However, their electrochem. performance is still severely impaired by structural aggregation and fracture during the conversion reaction.  To address these issues, various methodologies for the fabrication of hierarchical and hybrid nanostructures, with optimization of materials and electrodes, have been fully investigated and reviewed.  As regards tuning the TMD-based materials, extensive efforts have been undertaken toward optimization of their intrinsic structure at the at. level, including surface defects, interlayer spacing expansion, phase control, alloying, and heteroatom doping. This review provides a well-timed and crit. appraisal of recent advances in the engineering of TMDs at the at. level for AMIBs, by combining computational and exptl. approaches.         </div><div class="casAbstract">Layered transition metal dichalcogenides (TMDs), such as MoS2, WS2, MoSe2, WSe2, etc., have received considerable interest recently due to their unique properties and fascinating applications.                         rightTab-cas_cit17R"><div class="casContent"><span class="casTitleNuber">17</span><div class="casTitle"><span class="NLM_cas:atitle">Interface and Doping Effects on Li Ion Storage Behavior of Graphene/Li2O</span></div><div class="casAuthors">Wang, Tianshuai; Zhao, Naiqin; Shi, Chunsheng; Ma, Liying; He, Fang; He, Chunnian; Li, Jiajun; Liu, Enzuo</div><div class="citationInfo"><span class="NLM_cas:title">Journal of Physical Chemistry C</span>
        (<span class="NLM_cas:date">2017</span>),
    <span class="NLM_cas:volume">121</span>
        (<span class="NLM_cas:issue">36</span>),
    <span class="NLM_cas:pages">19559-19567</span>CODEN:
                <span class="NLM_cas:coden">JPCCCK</span>;

            (<span class="NLM_cas:orgname">American Chemical Society</span>)
        </div><div class="casAbstract">Graphene/metal oxide nanocomposites have been widely used as the anode materials for Li ion batteries, which exhibit much higher Li storage capacity beyond their theor. capacity.  In order to make clear the Li storage mechanism in graphene/metal oxide, we systematically investigated the interface and (B, N, O, S) doping effects on Li ion storage behavior in graphene/Li2O using first-principles total energy calcns.  It is revealed that the doping elements increase the van der Waals interface interaction of graphene/Li2O by changing the electronic structure of graphene through different mechanisms.  The Li storage at the graphene/Li2O interface exhibits the synergistic effect resulting from the enhanced interface interaction by the Li insertion at the interface.  The p-type and n-type doping induced by B and N dopants in graphene enhance and reduce the Li storage capability of graphene/Li2O, resp.  O and S doping result in the localization of the electronic states in graphene which benefits the Li adsorption at the interface.  The localization of electronic states combined with the appropriate dopant electronegativity can enhance the Li atoms adsorption and diffusion simultaneously.  Thereby, the highest interfacial lithium storage (0.330 mhA/m2) is obtained for the O-doped system, while the S-doped system possesses the good balance between interfacial Li storage (0.220 mhA/m2) and diffusion energy barrier (0.27 eV).  The results open a new insight for the design of graphene/metal oxide composites as energy storage materials.</div></div></div></div></div></div></li><li id="rightTab-ref18"><div class="reference"><strong class="refLabel"><a href="#ref18" class="rightTabRefNumLink">18</a></strong><div class="NLM_citation references__item" id="rightTab-cit18" data-doi="10.1002/aenm.201600467"><span><span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Gao, H.                         rightTab-cas_cit20R"><div class="casContent"><span class="casTitleNuber">20</span><div class="casTitle"><span class="NLM_cas:atitle">Sodiation Kinetics of Metal Oxide Conversion Electrodes: A Comparative Study with Lithiation</span></div><div class="casAuthors">He, Kai; Lin, Feng; Zhu, Yizhou; Yu, Xiqian; Li, Jing; Lin, Ruoqian; Nordlund, Dennis; Weng, Tsu-Chien; Richards, Ryan M.; Yang, Xiao-Qing; Doeff, Marca M.; Stach, Eric A.; Mo, Yifei; Xin, Huolin L.; Su, Dong</div><div class="citationInfo"><span class="NLM_cas:title">Nano Letters</span>
        (<span class="NLM_cas:date">2015</span>),
    <span class="NLM_cas:volume">15</span>
        (<span class="NLM_cas:issue">9</span>),
    <span class="NLM_cas:pages">5755-5763</span>CODEN:
                <span class="NLM_cas:coden">NALEFD</span>;
        ISSN:<span class="NLM_cas:issn">1530-6984</span>.
    
            (<span class="NLM_cas:orgname">American Chemical Society</span>)
        </div><div class="casAbstract">The development of sodium ion batteries (NIBs) can provide an alternative to lithium ion batteries (LIBs) for sustainable, low-cost energy storage.  However, due to the larger size and higher m/e ratio of the sodium ion compared to lithium, sodiation reactions of candidate electrodes are expected to differ in significant ways from the corresponding lithium ones.  In this work, we investigated the sodiation mechanism of a typical transition metal-oxide, NiO, through a set of correlated techniques, including electrochem. and synchrotron studies, real-time electron microscopy observation, and ab initio mol. dynamics (MD) simulations.  We found that a cryst. Na2O reaction layer that was formed at the beginning of sodiation plays an important role in blocking the further transport of sodium ions.  In addn., sodiation in NiO exhibits a "shrinking-core" mode that results from a layer-by-layer reaction, as identified by ab initio MD simulations.  For lithiation, however, the formation of Li antisite defects significantly distorts the local NiO lattice that facilitates Li insertion, thus enhancing the overall reaction rate.  These observations delineate the mechanistic difference between sodiation and lithiation in metal-oxide conversion materials.  More importantly, our findings identify the importance of understanding the role of reaction layers on the functioning of electrodes and thus provide crit. insights into further optimizing NIB materials through surface engineering.</div></div></div></div></div></div></li><li id="rightTab-ref21"><div class="reference"><strong class="refLabel"><a href="#ref21" class="rightTabRefNumLink">21</a></strong><div class="NLM_citation references__item" id="rightTab-cit21" data-doi="10.1002/adma.201500196"><span><span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Zhang, K.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Hu, Z.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Liu, X.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Tao, Z.                 10.1002/adma.201500196</span> </span><div class=" links-group getFTR"><div class="getFTR__content getFTR__placeholder"></div><div class="casRecord" id="

        </div><div class="casAbstract">The use is demonstrated of FeSe2 as an anode material for sodium-ion batteries.  The FeSe2 microspheres assembled by nanooctahedra display a high tapping d. of 1.85 g cm-3 and a discharge capacity of 447 mA h g-1 at 0.1 A g-1.  The FeSe2 anode also exhibits long-term cyclic stability (372 mA h g-1 with capacity retention of 89.0% after 2000 cycles at 1 A g-1 ) and excellent high-rate performance 388 mA h g-1 at 10 C, 337 mA h g-1 at 20 C, 226 mA h g-1 at 50 C, where 1 C = 0.5 A g-1.  The conversion reaction of FeSe2 + 4Na+ + 4e ↔ Fe + 2Na2Se has been confirmed.  Furthermore, the FeSe2 anode is coupled with an excessive Na3V2(PO4)3 cathode (the capacity of the Na3V2(PO4)3 is 1.5-2 times more than that of FeSe2) to fabricate a full cell, which exhibits a discharge capacity of 366 mA h g-1 based on the mass of FeSe2 with an av. output voltage of about 1.7 V at 1 A g-1.</div></div></div></div></div></div></li><li id="rightTab-ref22"><div class="reference"><strong class="refLabel"><a href="#ref22" class="rightTabRefNumLink">22</a></strong><div class="NLM_citation references__item" id="rightTab-cit22" data-doi="10.1002/adfm.201402833"><span><span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Zhang, Y.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Zhu, P.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Huang, L.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Xie, J.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Zhang, S.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Cao, G.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Zhao, X. Funct.         (<span class="NLM_cas:date">2015</span>),
    <span class="NLM_cas:volume">25</span>

        </div><div class="casAbstract">Na-ion batteries have been considered as promising alternatives to Li-ion batteries due to the natural abundance of sodium resources.  Searching for high-performance anode materials currently becomes a hot topic and also a great challenge for developing Na-ion batteries.  In this work, a novel hybrid anode is synthesized consisting of ultrafine, few-layered SnS2 anchored on few-layered reduced graphene oxide (rGO) by a facile solvothermal route.  The SnS2/rGO hybrid exhibits a high capacity, ultralong cycle life, and superior rate capability.  The hybrid can deliver a high charge capacity of 649 mA-h/g at 100 mA/g.  At 800 mA/g (1.8C), it can yield an initial charge capacity of 469 mA-h/g, which can be maintained at 89% and 61%, resp., after 400 and 1000 cycles.  The hybrid can also sustain a c.d. up to 12.8 A/g (≈28C) where the charge process can be completed in only 1.3 min while still delivering a charge capacity of 337 mA-h/g.         </div><div class="casAbstract">Room-temp. sodium-ion batteries have attracted increased attention for energy storage due to the natural abundance of sodium.  However, it remains a huge challenge to develop versatile electrode materials with favorable properties, which requires smart structure design and good mechanistic understanding.  Herein, we reported a general and scalable approach to synthesize three-dimensional (3D) titania-graphene hybrid via electrostatic-interaction-induced self-assembly.  Synchrotron X-ray probe, transmission electron microscopy, and computational modeling revealed that the strong interaction between titania and graphene through comparably strong van der Waals forces not only facilitates bulk Na+ intercalation but also enhances the interfacial sodium storage.  As a result, the titania-graphene hybrid exhibits exceptional long-term cycle stability up to 5000 cycles, and ultrahigh rate capability up to 20 C for sodium storage.  Furthermore, d. function theory calcn. indicated that the interfacial Li+, K+, Mg2+, and Al3+ storage can be enhanced as well.  The proposed general strategy opens up new avenues to create versatile materials for advanced battery systems.</div></div></div></div></div></div></li><li id="rightTab-ref25"><div class="reference"><strong class="refLabel"><a href="#ref25" class="rightTabRefNumLink">25</a></strong><div class="NLM_citation references__item" id="rightTab-cit25" data-doi="10.1016/j.jechem.2018.09.001"><span><span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Huang, J.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Wei, Z.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Liao, J.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Ni, W.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Wang, C.
